Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
8 min
Hierarchy Among Hanafi Imams in Fatwa
List of principles for preferring one Hanafi imam over another in fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which Hanafi imam's opinion is mufta bih in ibadat, qada, wirathah, and identify the seventeen issues where Imam Zufar's opinion is preferred.

Core Idea
In Hanafi iftaa, Imam A'zam rules ibadat, Abu Yusuf rules qada, Muhammad rules wirathah, and Zufar rules seventeen exceptions.

This lesson introduces the foundational hierarchy of authority among the Hanafi imams for issuing fatwas. When the great imams differ, the mufti must know whose opinion to prefer based on the chapter of fiqh. This hierarchy is a key part of Rasm al-Mufti.

Guided Path
1
Identify the applicable chapter
Determine which chapter of fiqh the case falls into: ibadat, qada, wirathah, or other. This decides which imam's opinion is primary.
For chapters not specified, generally Imam A'zam's opinion is mufta bih unless overridden by other principles.
2
Apply the imam-specific rule
Use the principles: ibadat → Imam A'zam; qada → Abu Yusuf; wirathah → Muhammad; seventeen issues → Zufar. For other chapters with ikhtilaf, check further rules.
If the ikhtilaf is based on change of time (principle 6 and 7), the later rule may apply.
3
Consider ikhtilaf based on time
If the disagreement between Imam A'zam and the Sahibayn stems from changing times, the later opinion may be given weight. Principle 6 and 7 guide this.
Principle 6: ikhtilaf based on time → adopt the later. Principle 7: not based on time → follow the standard hierarchy.
4
Identify the mufta bih
After applying the above, determine the mufta bih ruling for fatwa. This is the operative position.
Use the hierarchy: Imam A'zam > Sahibayn unless exceptions apply.

آٹھواں اصول: امام اعظم کے شاگردوں کے اقوال پر فتوی دینا .
نواں اصول : مشائخ کے اقوال پر فتوی دینا ۔
دسواں اصول: عبادات میں امام اعظم کا قول مفتی بہ ہے
گیارہواں اصول: قضاء کے باب میں امام ابو یوسف کا قول مفتی بہ ہے
بارہواں اصول: وراثت میں امام محمد کا قول مفتی بہ ہے تیرہواں اصول : سترہ مسائل میں امام زفر کا قول مفتی یہ ہے

پانچواں اصول : شروح کے مسائل فتاوی پر مقدم ہوں گے
چھنا اصول : فقہی کتا ہیں اصولی کتابوں پر مقدم ہوں گی

دوسرا اصول : مجتہد کا جو قول بظاہر حدیث کے خلاف ہو
تیسرا اصول : استحسان کو قیاس پر ترجیح حاصل ہوگی۔
چوتھا اصول: ترجیح صریح التزامی تصیح پر مقدم ہوگی

پہلا اسلوب : راجح قول کو مقدم کرنا .
دوسرا اسلوب : راجح قول کی دلیل کو مؤخر کرنا
تیسرا اسلوب صرف راجح قول کی دلیل ذکر کرنا.
چوتھا اسلوب : غیر راجح قول کی تردید کرنا۔
پانچواں اسلوب : جب کسی مسئلہ میں تین اقوال ہوں ۔

آٹھواں اصول : مصنف کا کسی ایک فقیہ کی تصحیح کو اصح قرار دینا

دوسرا اصول : فتوی والا قول صحیح کے مقابلہ میں رائج ہوگا
تیسرا اصول: امام اعظم کا قول راجح ہوگا ۔
چوتھا اصول : ظاہر الروایہ کا قول راجح ہوگا
پانچواں اصول : متون کا قول شروح و فتاوی پر رائج ہوگا
شر عقود رسم المف
Naseem from al-Mufti in hal mubad. Fifth principle: When there is ikhtilaf among Hanafis in a matter. Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti rasm. Sixth principle: When the ikhtilaf between Imam A'zam and the Sahibayn is based on the change of time and era. Seventh principle: When their ikhtilaf is not based on the change of time. Eighth principle: Giving fatwa based on the statements of Imam A'zam's students. Ninth principle: Giving fatwa based on the statements of the mashayikh. Tenth principle: In acts of worship, Imam A'zam's statement is mufta bih. Eleventh principle: In the chapter of qada, Imam Abu Yusuf's statement is mufta bih. Twelfth principle: In inheritance, Imam Muhammad's statement is mufta bih. Thirteenth principle: In seventeen issues, Imam Zufar's statement is mufta bih. Six principles of preference based on books. First principle: It is wajib to give fatwa based on Zahir al-Riwayah. Second principle: In Zahir al-Riwayah, the last compilation will be relied upon. Third principle: Giving fatwa based on non-Zahir al-Riwayah. Fourth principle: Issues from the mutun take precedence over shuruh and fatawa. Fifth principle: Issues from shuruh take precedence over fatawa. Sixth principle: Fiqh books take precedence over usul books. Five principles of preference based on evidence. First principle: When a saying is based on a well-known hadith. Second principle: The saying of a mujtahid that apparently contradicts a hadith. Third principle: Istihsan takes precedence over qiyas. Fourth principle: Preference is given to explicit over implied. Fifth principle: It is necessary to follow the style of the authors. First style: Preferring the rajih saying. Second style: Delaying the evidence of the rajih saying. Third style: Mentioning only the evidence of the rajih saying. Fourth style: Refuting the non-rajih saying. Fifth style: When there are three sayings in a matter. Ten principles of preference based on words of preference. First principle: When the word 'sahih' is used collectively. Second principle: When the correction of a saying is done with the word 'fatwa'. Third principle: When both sayings are preferred with the word 'fatwa'. Fourth principle: When the status of the correcting scholars is equal. Fifth principle: When the status of the correcting scholars differs. Sixth principle: When the words 'sahih' and 'asahh' are used for what is correct. Seventh principle: The same Imam calling one saying 'sahih' and another 'asahh'. Eighth principle: The author declaring the correction of a particular faqih as 'asahh'. Ninth principle: When the superlative form is used for correction. Tenth principle: When only one saying is corrected. Third principle: Fourteen principles of harmonizing the preferences of the mashayikh. First principle: 'Asahh' is preferred over 'sahih'. ... Second principle: The saying with 'fatwa' is prevalent over 'sahih'. Third principle: Imam A'zam's saying is rajih. Fourth principle: The saying from Zahir al-Riwayah is rajih. Fifth principle: The saying from mutun is prevalent over shuruh and fatawa. Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of principles (usul) that a Hanafi mufti must apply when faced with conflicting rulings within the madhhab. It is organized into several categories: (1) preference among the Hanafi imams themselves (Imam Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar) in various chapters of fiqh, (2) preference based on the type of book (Zahir al-Riwayah, mutun, shuruh, fatawa, usul), (3) preference based on the strength of evidence (mashhur hadith, appearance of contradiction, istihsan, explicit vs. implicit, author's style), (4) styles of presenting preferred rulings, (5) preference based on wording indicating correction (sahih, asahh, fatwa), and (6) rules for harmonizing the preferences of later mashayikh. Each principle provides a clear rule for the mufti to determine which ruling to give in fatwa. For example, in matters of worship, Imam Abu Hanifa's opinion is given precedence, while in judicial matters, Imam Abu Yusuf's opinion is preferred, and in inheritance, Imam Muhammad's. The mufti must also prioritize Zahir al-Riwayah rulings, istihsan over qiyas, and explicit over implicit. These principles form the backbone of Hanafi fatwa methodology.

Key Terms
مفتی بہ (Mufta bih): The ruling that is given in fatwa; the operative opinion in the madhhab.
راجح (Rajih): The preferred or stronger opinion among conflicting views.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which chapter of fiqh is Imam Abu Yusuf's opinion mufta bih?
A) Ibadat (Acts of worship)
B) Qada (Judicial matters)
C) Wirathah (Inheritance)
D) All chapters

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufti must apply a chapter-specific hierarchy of imams: Imam A'zam for ibadat, Abu Yusuf for qada, Muhammad for wirathah, and Zufar for seventeen issues.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
10 min
Book-Based Preference in Hanafi Fatwa
List of six principles for preferring rulings based on the type of book

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the hierarchy of Hanafi books: Zahir al-Riwayah, mutun, shuruh, fatawa, and usul books, to determine the preferred ruling.

Core Idea
Zahir al-Riwayah is the most authoritative; within it, the latest compilation prevails; next come mutun, then shuruh, then fatawa, then usul.

Hanafi fatwa methodology prescribes a clear hierarchy of books. Rulings from Zahir al-Riwayah are obligatory to follow, and conflicts between books are resolved by this hierarchy. This lesson covers the six principles of book-based preference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source book of each opinion
Determine whether the opinion is found in a book of Zahir al-Riwayah, a matn, a sharh, a fatwa collection, or a usul work.
Zahir al-Riwayah includes the six books of Imam Muhammad.
2
Apply the hierarchy
Check the order: (1) Zahir al-Riwayah > (2) later compilations within Zahir al-Riwayah > (3) mutun > (4) shuruh > (5) fatawa > (6) usul books. Prefer the opinion from the higher category.
If two opinions are from the same category, use additional principles (e.g., last compilation).
3
Check for the last compilation rule
Within Zahir al-Riwayah, the later compilation (e.g., al-Ziyadat over al-Mabsut) is given preference, as per principle 2.
This is because later works refine and sometimes supersede earlier ones.
4
Consider non-Zahir al-Riwayah
If a ruling is not in Zahir al-Riwayah, it can still be used under certain conditions (principle 3), but it is less preferred.
The mufti may adopt a non-Zahir al-Riwayah ruling if it is stronger in evidence or required by necessity.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of principles (usul) that a Hanafi mufti must apply when faced with conflicting rulings within the madhhab. It is organized into several categories: (1) preference among the Hanafi imams themselves (Imam Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar) in various chapters of fiqh, (2) preference based on the type of book (Zahir al-Riwayah, mutun, shuruh, fatawa, usul), (3) preference based on the strength of evidence (mashhur hadith, appearance of contradiction, istihsan, explicit vs. implicit, author's style), (4) styles of presenting preferred rulings, (5) preference based on wording indicating correction (sahih, asahh, fatwa), and (6) rules for harmonizing the preferences of later mashayikh. Each principle provides a clear rule for the mufti to determine which ruling to give in fatwa. For example, in matters of worship, Imam Abu Hanifa's opinion is given precedence, while in judicial matters, Imam Abu Yusuf's opinion is preferred, and in inheritance, Imam Muhammad's. The mufti must also prioritize Zahir al-Riwayah rulings, istihsan over qiyas, and explicit over implicit. These principles form the backbone of Hanafi fatwa methodology.

Key Terms
متن (Matn): A concise text of fiqh, usually a primer like al-Mukhtasar al-Quduri or Kanz al-Daqa'iq.
شرح (Sharh): A commentary on a matn, e.g., al-Hidayah on al-Quduri.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the book-based hierarchy, which type of book takes precedence after Zahir al-Riwayah?
A) Fatawa
B) Shuruh
C) Mutun
D) Usul books

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufti must always prefer rulings from Zahir al-Riwayah, then mutun, then shuruh, then fatawa, then usul books.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Evidence-Based Preference and Istihsan
List of five principles for preferring rulings based on evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the five evidence-based principles of tarjih, including the priority of istihsan over qiyas.

Core Idea
A ruling based on a well-known hadith is preferred; istihsan overrides qiyas, and explicit evidence beats implied.

When the Hanafi school has multiple opinions, the mufti must consider the strength of evidence. This lesson covers five principles that prioritize rulings based on hadith, address apparent contradiction, and establish that istihsan takes precedence over qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Assess the evidence for each opinion
Determine if any opinion is based on a well-known (mashhur) hadith, or if it appears to contradict a hadith, or if it uses istihsan vs qiyas.
Mashhur hadith is a hadith that is widely transmitted but not mutawatir.
2
Apply the hadith-based rules
Principle 1: If an opinion is based on a well-known hadith, it is preferred. Principle 2: If a mujtahid's opinion apparently contradicts a hadith, it may still be given if there is a valid reason.
The second principle requires the mujtahid to have a justification, such as abrogation or interpretation.
3
Apply istihsan and qiyas rules
Principle 3: Istihsan is preferred over qiyas. This is a core Hanafi method. Principle 4: Explicit (sarih) evidence is preferred over implied (iltizami).
Istihsan is a departure from qiyas for a stronger reason.
4
Follow the author's style
Principle 5: It is necessary to follow the style of the authors (e.g., if the author presents the evidence of the rajih opinion first, the mufti should follow that pattern).
This ensures consistency with how the madhhab is presented in its authoritative texts.

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of principles (usul) that a Hanafi mufti must apply when faced with conflicting rulings within the madhhab. It is organized into several categories: (1) preference among the Hanafi imams themselves (Imam Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar) in various chapters of fiqh, (2) preference based on the type of book (Zahir al-Riwayah, mutun, shuruh, fatawa, usul), (3) preference based on the strength of evidence (mashhur hadith, appearance of contradiction, istihsan, explicit vs. implicit, author's style), (4) styles of presenting preferred rulings, (5) preference based on wording indicating correction (sahih, asahh, fatwa), and (6) rules for harmonizing the preferences of later mashayikh. Each principle provides a clear rule for the mufti to determine which ruling to give in fatwa. For example, in matters of worship, Imam Abu Hanifa's opinion is given precedence, while in judicial matters, Imam Abu Yusuf's opinion is preferred, and in inheritance, Imam Muhammad's. The mufti must also prioritize Zahir al-Riwayah rulings, istihsan over qiyas, and explicit over implicit. These principles form the backbone of Hanafi fatwa methodology.

Key Terms
استحسان (Istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides analogy for a stronger legal basis.
قیاس (Qiyas): Analogical reasoning from a source case to a new case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the evidence-based principles, which takes precedence when there is a conflict between an opinion based on istihsan and one based on qiyas?
A) The opinion based on qiyas
B) The opinion based on istihsan
C) Both are equal
D) It depends on the book hierarchy

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi tarjih, istihsan overrides qiyas, explicit evidence beats implied, and opinions backed by well-known hadith are preferred.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
advanced
10 min
Principles of Wording in Tarjih
List of ten principles for preferring rulings based on specific wording used by scholars

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to interpret the words of preference (sahih, asahh, fatwa) used by Hanafi scholars to identify the rajih opinion.

Core Idea
Words like 'sahih', 'asahh', and 'fatwa' signal the relative strength of opinions; 'asahh' beats 'sahih', and 'fatwa' beats 'sahih' in many contexts.

When Hanafi scholars use specific terms to indicate preference, the mufti must understand their weight. This lesson covers ten principles that clarify the meaning of terms like 'sahih', 'asahh', and 'fatwa', and how to resolve conflicts when multiple scholars use these terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms used
Look for key words: 'sahih' (correct), 'asahh' (more correct), 'fatwa' (verdict), 'rajih' (preferred), 'a'lam' (most knowing), etc.
These words indicate the author's assessment of the strength of an opinion.
2
Apply the priority hierarchy of terms
Principle 1: When 'sahih' is used collectively, it indicates the correct opinion. Principle 2: When a saying is corrected with the word 'fatwa', it is given weight. Principle 3: If both sayings are called 'fatwa', further rules apply.
The term 'asahh' is stronger than 'sahih' (see later principles).
3
Consider the status of the correcting scholars
Principles 4-5: If the scholars who corrected the opinion are of equal status, both opinions are considered; if different, the correction by the higher-status scholar prevails.
Status is determined by scholarly rank and reputation.
4
Handle superlative forms
Principles 6-10 deal with use of 'sahih' and 'asahh', and when only one opinion is corrected. The superlative (asahh) indicates a stronger preference.
If the same imam calls one opinion 'sahih' and another 'asahh', the latter is preferred.

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of principles (usul) that a Hanafi mufti must apply when faced with conflicting rulings within the madhhab. It is organized into several categories: (1) preference among the Hanafi imams themselves (Imam Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar) in various chapters of fiqh, (2) preference based on the type of book (Zahir al-Riwayah, mutun, shuruh, fatawa, usul), (3) preference based on the strength of evidence (mashhur hadith, appearance of contradiction, istihsan, explicit vs. implicit, author's style), (4) styles of presenting preferred rulings, (5) preference based on wording indicating correction (sahih, asahh, fatwa), and (6) rules for harmonizing the preferences of later mashayikh. The mufti must also prioritize Zahir al-Riwayah rulings, istihsan over qiyas, and explicit over implicit. These principles form the backbone of Hanafi fatwa methodology.

Key Terms
صحيح (Sahih): Correct; used to indicate that an opinion is deemed valid by the scholar.
أصح (Asahh): More correct; a stronger term than sahih, indicating a higher degree of correctness.
فتوى (Fatwa): A legal verdict; when an opinion is described as 'fatwa', it means it is the operative opinion for giving verdicts.

A) The one called sahih
B) The one called asahh

D) Only the one called fatwa

Key Takeaway
The terms 'sahih', 'asahh', and 'fatwa' have specific weights; 'asahh' is stronger than 'sahih', and 'fatwa' often indicates the operative ruling.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Harmonizing Preferences of Later Mashayikh
List of first five principles for harmonizing the preferences of later Hanafi mashayikh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principles for harmonizing conflicting preferences among later Hanafi scholars (mashayikh), using the rules of precedence based on wording and authority.

Core Idea
Later scholars' preferences are resolved by a hierarchy: 'asahh' over 'sahih', 'fatwa' over 'sahih', Imam A'zam's view over others, Zahir al-Riwayah over later, and mutun over shuruh.

When later Hanafi scholars (mashayikh) express different preferences, the mufti must apply specific rules to harmonize them. This lesson covers the first five principles from the third section of the list, which provide a clear order: 'asahh' beats 'sahih', the opinion labeled 'fatwa' beats the one labeled 'sahih', Imam A'zam's view is rajih, Zahir al-Riwayah views are rajih, and mutun views prevail over shuruh and fatawa.

Guided Path
1
Identify if the preferences come from mashayikh
Determine that the conflicting opinions are from later scholars (mashayikh) and not from the founding imams. These principles apply specifically to harmonizing their preferences.
Mashayikh include scholars like al-Haskafi, Ibn Abidin, and others from the later period.
2
Apply the first principle: asahh vs sahih
If one scholar declares a view 'asahh' and another declares it 'sahih', the 'asahh' view is preferred. This is principle 1 of this set.
This aligns with the wording-based principles but is specific to mashayikh.
3
Apply the second principle: fatwa vs sahih
If one scholar says a view is 'fatwa' (the operative for verdicts) and another says it is 'sahih', the 'fatwa' view is prevalent.
This shows that operational usability (fatwa) outweighs mere correctness (sahih).
4
Apply the third and fourth principles: priority of Imam A'zam and Zahir al-Riwayah
Imam A'zam's own saying is rajih (principle 3). Also, the saying from Zahir al-Riwayah is rajih (principle 4). These may override later preferences.
These principles tie back to earlier hierarchies, ensuring consistency.
5
Apply the fifth: mutun over shuruh and fatawa
The saying from the mutun is prevalent over those from shuruh and fatawa. This reinforces the book hierarchy.
This prevents later commentaries from superseding the foundational texts.

شر عقود رسم المف
Naseem from al-Mufti in hal mubad. Fifth principle: When there is ikhtilaf among Hanafis in a matter. Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti rasm. Sixth principle: When the ikhtilaf between Imam A'zam and the Sahibayn is based on the change of time and era. Seventh principle: When their ikhtilaf is not based on the change of time. Eighth principle: Giving fatwa based on the statements of Imam A'zam's students. Ninth principle: Giving fatwa based on the statements of the mashayikh. Tenth principle: In acts of worship, Imam A'zam's statement is mufta bih. Eleventh principle: In the chapter of qada, Imam Abu Yusuf's statement is mufta bih. Twelfth principle: In inheritance, Imam Muhammad's statement is mufta bih. Thirteenth principle: In seventeen issues, Imam Zufar's statement is mufta bih. Six principles of preference based on books. First principle: It is wajib to give fatwa based on Zahir al-Riwayah. Second principle: In Zahir al-Riwayah, the last compilation will be relied upon. Third principle: Giving fatwa based on non-Zahir al-Riwayah. Fourth principle: Issues from the mutun take precedence over shuruh and fatawa. Fifth principle: Issues from shuruh take precedence over fatawa. Sixth principle: Fiqh books take precedence over usul books. Five principles of preference based on evidence. First principle: When a saying is based on a well-known hadith. Second principle: The saying of a mujtahid that apparently contradicts a hadith. Third principle: Istihsan takes precedence over qiyas. Fourth principle: Preference is given to explicit over implied. Fifth principle: It is necessary to follow the style of the authors. First style: Preferring the rajih saying. Second style: Delaying the evidence of the rajih saying. Third style: Mentioning only the evidence of the rajih saying. Fourth style: Refuting the non-rajih saying. Fifth style: When there are three sayings in a matter. Ten principles of preference based on words of preference. First principle: When the word 'sahih' is used collectively. Second principle: When the correction of a saying is done with the word 'fatwa'. Third principle: When both sayings are preferred with the word 'fatwa'. Fourth principle: When the status of the correcting scholars is equal. Fifth principle: When the status of the correcting scholars differs. Sixth principle: When the words 'sahih' and 'asahh' are used for what is correct. Seventh principle: The same Imam calling one saying 'sahih' and another 'asahh'. Eighth principle: The author declaring the correction of a particular faqih as 'asahh'. Ninth principle: When the superlative form is used for correction. Tenth principle: When only one saying is corrected. Third principle: Fourteen principles of harmonizing the preferences of the mashayikh. First principle: 'Asahh' is preferred over 'sahih'. ... Second principle: The saying with 'fatwa' is prevalent over 'sahih'. Third principle: Imam A'zam's saying is rajih. Fourth principle: The saying from Zahir al-Riwayah is rajih. Fifth principle: The saying from mutun is prevalent over shuruh and fatawa. Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of principles (usul) that a Hanafi mufti must apply when faced with conflicting rulings within the madhhab. It is organized into several categories: (1) preference among the Hanafi imams themselves (Imam Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar) in various chapters of fiqh, (2) preference based on the type of book (Zahir al-Riwayah, mutun, shuruh, fatawa, usul), (3) preference based on the strength of evidence (mashhur hadith, appearance of contradiction, istihsan, explicit vs. implicit, author's style), (4) styles of presenting preferred rulings, (5) preference based on wording indicating correction (sahih, asahh, fatwa), and (6) rules for harmonizing the preferences of later mashayikh. Each principle provides a clear rule for the mufti to determine which ruling to give in fatwa. For example, in matters of worship, Imam Abu Hanifa's opinion is given precedence, while in judicial matters, Imam Abu Yusuf's opinion is preferred, and in inheritance, Imam Muhammad's. The mufti must also prioritize Zahir al-Riwayah rulings, istihsan over qiyas, and explicit over implicit. These principles form the backbone of Hanafi fatwa methodology.

Key Terms
مشائخ (Mashayikh): Later Hanafi scholars (especially from the 10th century onwards) who authored fatawa and commentaries.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the harmonizing principles, which label is preferred: a saying labeled 'fatwa' or one labeled 'sahih'?
A) The one labeled sahih
B) The one labeled fatwa
C) Neither
D) Both are equal

Key Takeaway
Conflicting preferences among later Hanafi scholars are resolved by a hierarchy: asahh > sahih, fatwa > sahih, and priority to Imam A'zam, Zahir al-Riwayah, and mutun.

Five Styles of Presenting the Preferred Ruling in Fatwa
Lists five styles of fatwa presentation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the five Hanafi styles of presenting the preferred ruling (rājiḥ) in a fatwa and select the appropriate style for a given scenario.

Core Idea
The mufti has five distinct styles to present the rājiḥ – each conveys authority differently.

In Hanafi fatwa methodology, presenting the preferred ruling is not a one-size-fits-all task. The classical scholars outlined five styles that a mufti can adopt depending on the context, the questioner, and the need for emphasis. This lesson walks you through each style and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
List the five styles from the text
Read the first part of the source passage and write down the five styles in order: (1) presenting the rājiḥ directly, (2) delaying its proof, (3) mentioning only its proof, (4) refuting the non-preferred statement, (5) handling three opinions.
The fifth style may include a separate rule for three opinions.
2
Understand the purpose of each style
For each style, consider when a mufti would use it. For example, style 1 is straightforward; style 2 may be used when the questioner needs gradual explanation; style 3 focuses on evidence; style 4 is polemical; style 5 is for multi-opinion issues.
The text does not elaborate on contexts; you must infer from general fiqh methodology.
3
Apply to a hypothetical fatwa
Imagine a mufti is asked about a sale contract with a known Hanafi rājiḥ. Choose the best style if the questioner is a scholar (style 3) vs. a layman (style 1). Justify your choice.
The Hanafi tradition values clarity and authority; the mufti's judgment is key.

Third style: Mentioning only the proof of the preferred statement. Fourth style: Refuting the non-preferred statement. Fifth style: When there are three statements in a issue. Commentary
This passage is a set of rules for a mufti on how to present and prefer rulings. It first outlines five styles of delivering a fatwa: presenting the preferred view outright, delaying its proof, mentioning only the proof, refuting the weaker view, and handling three views. Then it lists ten principles of preference (tarjih) based on the wording used by scholars. These principles help a mufti determine which ruling is more authoritative when the language of correction (like 'sahih', 'asahh', 'fatwa') varies. The principles consider the collective usage, the term 'fatwa', the rank of scholars, and the use of comparative or superlative forms. The mufti must apply these systematically.

Key Terms
اسلوب (uslūb): A style or method of presenting the ruling in a fatwa.
راجح قول (rājiḥ qawl): The preferred or stronger statement among multiple opinions.
تصحیح (taṣḥīḥ): The act of declaring a statement as correct (sahih) or more correct (asahh).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A Hanafi mufti must master five distinct styles of presenting the rājiḥ to effectively communicate the fatwa according to the context.

Ten Principles of Tarjih Based on Wording
Lists ten principles of tarjih based on wording.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the ten Hanafi principles of preference (tarjih) based on the wording used in scholarly corrections (taṣḥīḥ) to determine the more authoritative ruling.

Core Idea
The words used to correct a ruling—like 'fatwa', 'sahih', 'asahh'—carry hierarchical weight for the mufti.

When Hanafi scholars correct or prefer a ruling, they use specific terminology that a mufti must interpret correctly. This lesson unpacks ten principles that serve as a grammar for preference, covering collective usage, the word 'fatwa', the rank of scholars, and the comparative/superlative forms. Mastery of these principles is essential for issuing sound fatwas.

Guided Path
1
Enumerate the ten principles from the text
Read the second part of the passage and list all ten principles in order. Understand that they are not exhaustive but cover common scenarios.
The Arabic text may have slight OCR errors; cross-check with translation.
2
Categorize the principles by type
Group the principles: those based on the wording itself (e.g., using 'fatwa', 'asahh') and those based on the rank of scholars (principles 4 and 5).
Principles 1-3 and 6-10 deal with words; principles 4-5 deal with scholar hierarchy.
3
Apply to a hypothetical correction scenario
Suppose two scholars corrected the same issue: one says 'this is sahih' and another says 'this is asahh'. According to principle 6, which correction carries more weight? Explain why.
Remember: 'asahh' indicates a stronger correctness than 'sahih'.
4
Understand the fifth principle on differing ranks
When scholars of different ranks correct, the higher-ranking scholar's correction is given precedence (principle 5). This reflects the hierarchical nature of Hanafi scholarship.
This principle is an exception to the pure wording-based rules.

The Fourteen Principles for Reconciling Scholars' Preferences
Lists 14 principles for tarjih in fatwa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the fourteen Hanafi principles for tarjih to determine the mufta bihi ruling when conflicting opinions exist.

Core Idea
The mufti has 14 principles to choose the strongest opinion — from 'asahh' over 'sahih' to ease and maslahah.

This lesson presents the fourteen principles that a Hanafi mufti uses to reconcile conflicting statements among the later scholars (mashayikh). These principles serve as a systematic tarjih framework, ensuring fatwas are based on the most authoritative, beneficial, or well-supported opinion within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting opinions
First, note the two or more conflicting rulings that exist within the Hanafi school for a given issue.
2
Classify each opinion by its characteristics
For each opinion, determine whether it is labeled 'asahh' or 'sahih', whether it is from Imam Abu Hanifa or his students, whether it is from Zahir al-Riwayah or later works, whether it relies on istihsan or qiyas, whether it is easier for people, etc.
Use the fourteen principles as a checklist.
3
Apply the hierarchy from the principles
Apply the principles in order. For example, if one statement is 'asahh' and the other is 'sahih', the first principle favors 'asahh'. If one is from Imam Abu Hanifa and the other from Sahibayn, the third principle favors Imam Abu Hanifa.
Some principles may conflict; the mufti must weigh them using the overall hierarchy.
4
Select the mufta bihi ruling
After applying all relevant principles, determine which ruling is supported by the greatest number or strongest principles. This becomes the mufta bihi (operative ruling for fatwa).
The principles are not absolute but serve as guides; the qawida 'al-umur bi-maqasidiha' also applies.

Third principle: 14 principles for reconciling the preferences of the mashayikh. First principle: Asahh prevails over sahih. Second principle: The statement with fatwa will be prevalent over sahih. Third principle: The statement of Imam A'zam is preferable. Fourth principle: The statement of Zahir al-Riwaya is preferable. Fifth principle: The statement of the matn will be prevalent over sharh and fatawa. Sixth principle: The statement of the senior fuqaha is preferable. Seventh principle: Istihsan prevails over qiyas. Eighth principle: The preference that is most beneficial for the waqf is preferable. Ninth principle: The easier statement is preferable. Tenth principle: The statement based on strong evidence is preferable. Eleventh principle: The statement most beneficial for the poor is preferable. Twelfth principle: The statement that drops the hudud is preferable. Thirteenth principle: The statement that declares something prohibited is preferable. Fourteenth principle: The statement based on maslahah is preferable. An important benefit regarding reconciliation of the mashayikh's preferences. Fourth principle: Giving fatwa from reliable books. Fifth principle: Giving fatwa from unreliable books. Reasons for books being unreliable: First reason: The author being unknown. Second reason: The book containing weak statements. Third reason: The expression being extremely brief. Fourth reason: A verifying faqih declaring the book weak. Fifth reason: A verifying faqih declaring the author weak. Sixth reason: Doubt in attribution to the author. Seventh reason: The book being in other disciplines besides fiqh. Eighth reason: The book being rare. Ninth reason: In printing, many errors.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage continues the rules for a mufti. It provides fourteen principles for reconciling conflicting preferences among later scholars (mashayikh). Each principle indicates which type of opinion should be given precedence: e.g., 'asahh' over 'sahih', the opinion of Imam Abu Hanifa, rulings from Zahir al-Riwaya (the canonical six books), the texts of matn over commentaries, opinions of senior jurists, istihsan over qiyas, and opinions that are easier, benefit waqf or the poor, drop penal limits, declare prohibition, or are based on maslaha. Then it mentions an important benefit (not detailed in OCR) and moves to the fourth and fifth principles about giving fatwa from reliable versus unreliable books. It lists nine reasons that render a book unreliable: unknown author, presence of weak statements, extreme brevity, a verifier's condemnation of the book or author, doubtful attribution, being in non-fiqh disciplines, rarity, and many printing errors. A mufti must avoid basing fatwas on such books.

Key Terms
ظاہر الروایہ (Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah): The six canonical books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani: al-Mabsut, al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, al-Siyar al-Kabir, al-Ziyadat.
استحسان (istiḥsān): A juristic preference that overrides strict analogy (qiyas) due to a stronger consideration.
مصلحت (maṣlaḥah): Public interest or benefit, used as a basis for preference.

Key Terms from Source Passage

The mufti must systematically apply the fourteen principles of tarjih to ensure fatwas are consistent and authoritative.

Reliable vs Unreliable Books for Fatwa
Discusses reliable vs unreliable books and reasons for unreliability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the criteria that make a book reliable or unreliable for issuing fatwa in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A mufti must only use reliable books; nine reasons render a book unreliable for fatwa.

After establishing the principles for tarjih, the passage turns to the sources themselves. Not every book purporting to be Hanafi is acceptable for fatwa. This lesson outlines the nine reasons that disqualify a book, ensuring the mufti bases his rulings only on sound texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the book in question
When considering using a book for fatwa, first identify its author and content.
2
Check the nine reasons for unreliability
Go through each reason: unknown author, weak statements, extreme brevity, declared weak by a verifier, weak author, doubtful attribution, non-fiqh discipline, rarity, printing errors.
If any reason applies, the book should be avoided.
3
Determine if the book is reliable
If none of the nine reasons apply, the book can be considered reliable for fatwa, provided it is within the Hanafi tradition and recognized by scholars.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Principles of Preference in Fatwa (Rules 6–14)
Listing nine principles of preference in fatwa (rules 6–14).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the nine principles of tarjih (rules 6–14) when selecting between conflicting Hanafi opinions for fatwa.

Core Idea
A mufti prefers the opinion of senior jurists, istihsan over qiyas, benefit to waqf/poor, ease, strong evidence, averting hudud, prohibition, or maslahah.

In the Hanafi madhhab, multiple opinions often exist on a single issue. The early masters formulated specific principles to guide the mufti in choosing the most appropriate ruling. This passage presents the sixth through fourteenth such principles, each providing a distinct criterion for preference (tarjih). Mastery of these rules is essential for issuing sound fatwas and maintaining consistency within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Purpose of Tarjih
Tarjih is the process of preferring one legal opinion over another. Understand that these principles are tools to resolve apparent conflicts within the Hanafi madhhab.
The goal is not to reject other opinions but to select the most suitable for the context.
2
Memorize Each Principle by Number
The nine principles are numbered 6 through 14. Attach each number to its criterion: e.g., principle 6 = senior jurists, principle 7 = istihsan over qiyas, etc.
Grouping similar themes (e.g., benefit principles 8 & 11) can aid recall.
3
Apply the Principles to a Scenario
Given a hypothetical ikhtilaf, list conflicting opinions and evaluate which criterion or criteria apply. Determine the raajih opinion based on the principles.
Multiple principles may point to the same opinion; consider their relative weight if they conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
چھٹا اصول: اکابر فقہاء کا قول راجح ہوگا۔ ساتواں اصول: استحسان قیاس پر راجح ہوگا۔ آٹھواں اصول: انفع للوقف والی ترجیح راجح ہوگی۔ نواں اصول: بہ سہولت والا قول راجح ہوگا۔ دسواں اصول: قوی دلیل پر مبنی قول راجح ہوگا۔ گیارہواں اصول: انفع للفقراء والا قول راجح ہوگا۔ بارہواں اصول: حدود کو ساقط کرنے والا قول راجح ہوگا۔ تیرہواں اصول: حرمت والا قول راجح ہوگا۔ چودہواں اصول: مصلحت پر مبنی قول راجح ہوگا۔
Sixth principle: The saying of the senior jurists will be preponderant. Seventh principle: Istihsan will be preponderant over qiyas. Eighth principle: The preference that is more beneficial for the endowment will be preponderant. Ninth principle: The saying with ease will be preponderant. Tenth principle: A saying based on strong evidence will be preponderant. Eleventh principle: The saying more beneficial for the poor will be preponderant. Twelfth principle: The saying that averts hudud will be preponderant. Thirteenth principle: The saying of prohibition will be preponderant. Fourteenth principle: A saying based on public interest (maslahah) will be preponderant.
Source pages p. 7

Commentary
This passage presents nine rules of preference (tarjih) for a mufti when selecting between differing opinions within the Hanafi madhhab. The passage lists these as the sixth to fourteenth principles (the first five are not included here). The rules include preference for the opinion of senior jurists (akabir), for istihsan over qiyas, for what is more beneficial for waqf property or for the poor, for ease, for stronger evidence, for averting hudud punishments (since hudud are dropped by doubts), for prohibition (haram) over permissibility, and for maslahah (public interest). These rules guide a mufti when multiple conflicting opinions exist, helping him give the most appropriate fatwa.

Key Terms
راجح (raajih): Having tarjih, i.e., preponderant and given preference in fatwa
استحسان (istihsaan): Juristic preference; preferring a ruling that departs from analogical reasoning due to a stronger evidence or necessity

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The opinion that avoids applying hudud punishments
C) The opinion that agrees with the majority of later scholars
D) The opinion that is based on qiyas only

Key Takeaway
The mufti must master these nine tarjih principles to navigate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi madhhab and issue sound fatwas.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Obligation to Follow the Rajih or Zahir al-Riwayah
Ruling on which opinions a mufti must follow

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the hierarchy of authoritative opinions in Hanafi fatwa by identifying when a mufti must follow the rajih opinion or a Zahir al-Riwayah ruling.

Core Idea
A mufti is bound to follow either the preponderant opinion or the text of Zahir al-Riwayah unless a later rajih opposes it.

This passage from Rasm al-Mufti establishes the binding hierarchy of opinions for a Hanafi mufti. It clarifies that not all opinions in the madhhab are equal; the mufti must adhere to the opinion that has been given preponderance by qualified jurists (ahl al-tarjih) or, absent that, to the ruling recorded in the six core books of Zahir al-Riwayah. This ensures consistency and prevents arbitrary selection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sources of authority
Recognise that the rule mentions two bases: the rajih opinion (preponderant) and Zahir al-Riwayah (the core transmitted books).
2
Understand the role of Ahl al-Tarjih
Ahl al-Tarjih are the qualified scholars who determine which opinion is preponderant. Their decision carries authority.
They are mujtahid muftis in later Hanafi parlance.
3
Apply the priority order
First check if there is a known rajih opinion. If yes, follow it. If not, and the issue is recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah, follow that, unless a later tarjih contradicts.
The rule says 'or' implying order: rajih takes precedence over Zahir al-Riwayah.
4
Recognise the obligation
The rule uses 'wajib' (obligatory) – this is not optional. The mufti must adhere to this hierarchy.
This is a binding rule of iftaa methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعْلَمُ بأن الواجب اتِّبَاعُ مَا تَرْجِيْحُهُ عَنْ أَهْلِهِ قَدْ عُلِمَا ۹ أوْ كانَ ظَاهِرَ الرِّوَايَةِ وَلَمْ ١٠ يُرَجُحُوا خِلافَ ذَاكَ فَاعْلَمُ
ترجمہ: جان لو! کہ اس قول کی پیروی کرنا واجب ہے جس کا راج ہونا اصحاب الترجیح سے معلوم ہو چکا ہو، یا وہ مسئلہ ظاہر الروایہ میں مذکور ہو اور اصحاب الترجیح نے اس کے خلاف دوسرے قول کو راجح نہ قرار دیا ہو، پس اس سے آگاہ ہو جاؤ۔
بات دل نشیں ہوں سب جان لیں ضروری جو حکم دیں ہو راجح ہو پیروی اسی کی
ترجیح بھی انھیں کی جو اس کے اہل ٹھہریں ہیں ہیں کون اہل اس کے یہ بھی یہیں سمجھ لیں
ان میں سے ایک طبقہ ترجیح دے سکے گا جو فیصلہ ہو اس کا وہ ماننا پڑے گا
Know that it is obligatory to follow that whose preponderance (tarjih) from its people (ahl al-tarjih) is known, or that is in Zahir al-Riwayah and they have not given preponderance to its opposite. So know that. [Then Urdu translation and verses]
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This is a key rasm al-mufti rule. The author states the hierarchy of authoritative opinions: a mufti must follow either (1) the opinion that has been clearly established as preponderant (rajih) by the proper authorities (ahl al-tarjih, i.e., later mujtahids who weigh conflicting evidence), or (2) an opinion recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah (the six core books of Imam Muhammad) as long as the later authorities have not given preponderance to an opposing view. The Urdu verses reinforce that only those qualified (ahl al-tarjih) can decide preponderance, and their decision must be accepted.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani containing the established Hanafi rulings.
أهل الترجيح (Ahl al-Tarjih): The qualified scholars capable of weighing and determining the preponderant opinion in the madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which two types of opinions does the mufti have an obligation to follow?
A) Any opinion from a Hanafi text
B) The rajih opinion OR that in Zahir al-Riwayah
C) Only the opinion of Abu Hanifa
D) The opinion of the majority

Key Takeaway
A mufti's obligation is to follow the rajih opinion if known, otherwise the Zahir al-Riwayah ruling, ensuring adherence to the madhhab's strongest evidence.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
10 min
Obligation to Follow the Preponderant and Zahir al-Riwayah
Defining the wajib source for fatwa: tarjih and Zahir al-Riwayah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi fatwa hierarchy: identify a ruling as from Zahir al-Riwayah, Nawadir, or Nawazil, and determine the mufta bihi position using the rule of tarjih.

Core Idea
A mufti must follow either a rajih opinion or a ruling from Zahir al-Riwayah unless outweighed by tarjih.

This lesson introduces the foundational rasm al-mufti rule: the obligation to follow either a preponderant (rajih) opinion or a ruling from Zahir al-Riwayah. The passage establishes a clear hierarchy that ensures consistency and authority in fatwa. You will learn to distinguish between Zahir al-Riwayah, Nawadir, and Nawazil, and understand when tarjih overrides even the core texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two bases for fatwa
The passage states that it is wajib to follow either (1) a ruling whose preponderance has been established by scholars of tarjih, or (2) a ruling found in Zahir al-Riwayah provided that no contrary opinion has been given preponderance.
The term 'tarjih' means giving weight to one evidence over another.
2
Distinguish the three categories of Hanafi sources
Zahir al-Riwayah consists of six books by Imam Muhammad, transmitted with mutawatir chains. Nawadir are rare transmissions with weaker chains. Nawazil are later cases derived by mashayikh through deduction.
Memorize the six books: al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, al-Ziyadat, al-Mabsut.
3
Apply the condition for following Zahir al-Riwayah
A Zahir al-Riwayah ruling must be followed unless the scholars of tarjih have given preponderance to a contrary opinion. If they have, that contrary opinion becomes wajib.
This prevents blind adherence to the core texts when later tarjih indicates a stronger view.
4
Recognize the hierarchy's practical effect
The mufti must search first in Zahir al-Riwayah, then Nawadir, then Nawazil. But if tarjih exists for a contrary opinion, it overrides even Zahir al-Riwayah.
This hierarchy is unique to Hanafi usul al-iftaa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعْلَمُ بأن الواجب اتِّبَاعُ مَا تَرْجِيْحُهُ عَنْ أَهْلِهِ قَدْ عُلِمَا  أوْ كانَ ظَاهِرَ الرِّوَايَةِ وَلَمْ يُرَجُحُوا خِلافَ ذَاكَ فَاعْلَمُ ترجمہ: جان لو! کہ اس قول کی پیروی کرنا واجب ہے جس کا راج ہونا اصحاب الترجیح سے معلوم ہو چکا ہو، یا وہ مسئلہ ظاہر الروایہ میں مذکور ہو اور اصحاب الترجیح نے اس کے خلاف دوسرے قول کو راجح نہ قرار دیا ہو، پس اس سے آگاہ ہو جاؤ۔
وَكُتُبُ ظَاهِرِ الرِّوَايَات أَتَتْ سِئًا وَبِالأَصُولِ أيضاً سُمِّيَتُ صَنَّفها محمَّدُ الشَّيْبَانِيُّ حَرَّرَ فِيهَا الْمَذْهَبَ النُّعْمَانِي الْجَامِعُ الصَّغِيرُ وَ الْكَثِيرُ وَ السِّيرُ الكبيرُ وَالصَّغِيرُ ثُمَّ الزِّيَادَاتُ مَعَ الْمَبْسُوط تَوَاتَرَتْ بِالسَّنَدِ الْمَضْبُوط كَذَا لَهُ مَسَائِلُ النَّوَادِرُ إِسْنَادُهَا فِي الْكُتُبِ غَيْرُ ظَاهِرُ وَبَعْدَهَا مَسَائِلُ النَّوَازِلُ خَرَّجَهَا الْأَشْيَاحُ بِالدَّلَائِلُ
Know! It is obligatory to follow that which its preponderance from its people has been known, or that which is in Zahir al-Riwayah and they have not given preponderance to the contrary of that, so know. And the books of Zahir al-Riwayat are six, and they are also named al-Usul; Muhammad al-Shaybani authored them, in which he recorded the Nu'mani madhhab: al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, then al-Ziyadat and al-Mabsut. They are mutawatir with authentic chain. Likewise, he has the Masail al-Nawadir whose chain in the books is not apparent. And after them are the Masail al-Nawazil which the mashayikh derived with evidences.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage establishes the fundamental rasm rule of fatwa hierarchy in the Hanafi madhhab. The mufti is obliged to follow either a ruling that has been explicitly declared preponderant (rajih) by the scholars of tarjih, or a ruling that is found in the Zahir al-Riwayah corpus and has not been outweighed by a contrary opinion. Zahir al-Riwayah refers to the six core books authored by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani: al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, al-Ziyadat, and al-Mabsut. These are transmitted with strong, mutawatir chains. In contrast, the Nawadir (rare transmissions) have weaker chains and are not relied upon unless supported by later tarjih. After these are the Nawazil (new cases) derived by later scholars through deduction. The mufti must prioritize Zahir al-Riwayah; only when no ruling is found there may he turn to Nawadir, and only after that to Nawazil. This hierarchy ensures consistency and authority in fatwa.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The six books authored by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani that contain the core transmitted rulings of Abu Hanifa and his disciples, transmitted with strong chains.
النوادر (al-Nawadir): Rare transmissions from Imam Muhammad or others that are not included in the six books; their chains are less apparent.
النوازل (al-Nawazil): New legal cases derived by later scholars (mashayikh) through deduction and evidence, not directly from the transmitted corpus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a fatwa exists in Zahir al-Riwayah but the scholars of tarjih have given preponderance to a contrary opinion from Nawadir?
A) The mufti must follow the Zahir al-Riwayah opinion because it is from the six books.
B) The mufti must follow the contrary opinion that was given preponderance (rajih).
C) The mufti may choose either opinion.
D) The mufti must seek a third opinion from another madhhab.

Key Takeaway
A mufti must base his fatwa on either a rajih opinion or a Zahir al-Riwayah ruling; only if nothing is found there may he turn to Nawadir and then Nawazil.

The Six Books of Zahir al-Riwayah and Their Fatwa Hierarchy
Explaining the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah and their hierarchy in fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah, explain their composition order, and apply the hierarchy to determine which text is most authoritative in fatwa.

Core Idea
The six books of Zahir al-Riwayah are the primary sources; later compositions supersede earlier ones in fatwa authority.

This passage introduces the six canonical books of the Hanafi school authored by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani. These books form the foundation of fatwa. Understanding their hierarchy is essential for a mufti to know which text to rely upon when ruling.

Guided Path
1
List the six books
Recite the six names: al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, al-Siyar al-Kabir, al-Ziyadat, al-Mabsut.
These are also called al-Usul.
2
Understand the order of composition
Note that al-Mabsut (al-Asl) was written first, then al-Jami' al-Saghir, and finally al-Siyar al-Kabir.
The phrase 'wa akhir al-sittah tasnifan wared al-siyar al-kabir fahuwa al-mu'tamad' indicates the last is most relied upon.
3
Apply the hierarchy principle
Later works are more comprehensive and relied upon. Al-Siyar al-Kabir is the last and most authoritative.
Reverse composition order gives fatwa preference.
4
Distinguish Zahir al-Riwayah from Nawadir and Nawazil
Nawadir have weak chains; Nawazil are later scholarly deductions. Fatwa must start with Zahir al-Riwayah.
Nawazil are secondary and must be consistent with the six books.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَكُتُبُ ظَاهِرِ الرِّوَايَاتِ أَتَتْ سِتًّا وَبِالأُصُولِ أيضاً سُمِّيَتُ صَنَّفَها محمَّدُ الشَّيْبَانِيُّ حَرَّرَ فِيهَا الْمَذْهَبَ النُّعْمَانِي الْجَامِعُ الصَّغِيرُ وَالْكَبِيرُ وَالسِّيَرُ الْكَبِيرُ وَالصَّغِيرُ ثُمَّ الزِّيَادَاتُ مَعَ الْمَبْسُوطِ تَوَاتَرَتْ بِالسَّنَدِ الْمَضْبُوطِ كَذَا لَهُ مَسَائِلُ النَّوَادِرِ إِسْنَادُهَا فِي الْكُتُبِ غَيْرُ ظَاهِرٍ وَبَعْدَهَا مَسَائِلُ النَّوَازِلِ خَرَّجَهَا الْأَشْيَاحُ بِالدَّلَائِلِ. وَاشْتَهَرَ الْمَبْسُوطُ بِالأَصْلِ، وَذَا لِسَبْقِهِ السِّتَّةِ تَصْنِيفًا كَذَا الْجَامِعُ الصَّغِيرُ بَعْدَهُ فَمَا فِيهِ عَلَى الأَصْلِ لِذَا تَقَدَّمَا. وَآخِرُ السِّتَّةِ تَصْنِيفًا وَرَدَ السِّيَرُ الْكَبِيرُ فَهُوَ الْمُعْتَمَدُ.
The books of Zahir al-Riwayah are six and are also called 'al-Usul'. Muhammad al-Shaybani authored them, in which he codified the Nu'mani madhhab. [They are] al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Kabir and al-Saghir, then al-Ziyadat along with al-Mabsut. They are continuously transmitted with well-preserved chains. Likewise he has Masail al-Nawadir, whose chains in the books are not apparent. And after them are Masail al-Nawazil, which the shuyukh derived with proofs. And al-Mabsut is famous as 'al-Asl' because it preceded the six [in composition]. Similarly al-Jami' al-Saghir came after it, so whatever is in it relies on al-Asl for that reason, so it took precedence. And the last of the six in composition is al-Siyar al-Kabir, so it is the relied-upon [authority].
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a methodological statement about the hierarchy of texts in the Hanafi school. It identifies the six core books of Zahir al-Riwayah authored by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani: al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, al-Siyar al-Kabir, al-Ziyadat, and al-Mabsut. These are the primary transmitted sources (al-Usul) and are considered mutawatir in their chains. The passage then notes two additional categories: al-Nawadir, which have weak or non-apparent chains, and al-Nawazil, which are later deductions by scholars. It clarifies the order of composition: al-Mabsut was written first, hence called 'al-Asl'; al-Jami' al-Saghir came next and relies on al-Asl; and the last composed is al-Siyar al-Kabir, which is the most authoritative (mu'tamad) of the six. The order of composition also indicates a hierarchy: later texts are more comprehensive and relied upon in fatwa. The fatwa must be based on these six books first, then later compilations.

Key Terms
ظَاهِرُ الرِّوَايَةِ (Zahir al-Riwayah): The six books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani that contain the well-known transmitted rulings of the Hanafi school with reliable chains.
النَّوَادِرُ (al-Nawadir): Rare transmissions from Imam Muhammad with weak or unknown chains, not part of Zahir al-Riwayah.
النَّوَازِلُ (al-Nawazil): Later juristic cases derived by shuyukh using proofs, not directly from the founding texts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) al-Mabsut
C) al-Siyar al-Kabir
D) al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya

Key Takeaway
The six books of Zahir al-Riwayah are the foundation of Hanafi fatwa, with later composed texts taking precedence.

Hierarchy of the Six Books and Authority of Sarakhsi's Mabsut
Rasm al-mufti rule on book hierarchy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi hierarchy of the six Zahir al-Riwayah books to resolve conflicts between their rulings and identify the authoritative commentary for fatwa.

Core Idea
Later compositions override earlier ones; al-Sarakhsi's Mabsut is the final authority.

This passage from Naseem al-Mufti lays down a crucial rasm al-mufti rule: the hierarchy and precedence among the six core books of Zahir al-Riwayah by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for any mufti to give correct fatwas and to navigate apparent contradictions between primary texts. It also establishes that al-Sarakhsi's Mabsut is the most authoritative commentary, with no fatwa given against it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six books
List the books mentioned: al-Mabsut (also called al-Asl), al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Siyar al-Kabir, and al-Kafi as a compilation. Note that al-Mabsut here refers to Imam Muhammad's work, not Sarakhsi's commentary.
2
Understand the chronological order
The passage states: al-Mabsut was composed first, then al-Jami' al-Saghir, and finally al-Siyar al-Kabir. This order is the basis for precedence.
3
Apply the overruling principle
Later compositions take precedence. So al-Jami' al-Saghir overrides al-Mabsut, and al-Siyar al-Kabir overrides both. The passage explicitly says: 'what is in it (al-Jami' al-Saghir) takes precedence over al-Asl' and 'the last is the reliable one'.
4
Recognize the commentary authority
Al-Sarakhsi's Mabsut is the strongest commentary on al-Kafi. The passage declares: 'no action is taken contrary to it, and no deviation from it is made'. This makes it the final reference for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاشْتَهَرَ الْمَبْسُوطُ بِالأَصْلِ، وَذَا لِسَبْقِهِ السِّتَّةَ تَصْنِيفًا، كَذَا الْجَامِعُ الصَّغِيرُ بَعْدَهُ، فَمَا فِيهِ عَلَى الْأَصْلِ لِذَا تَقَدَّمَا. وَآخِرُ السِّتَّةِ تَصْنِيفًا وَرَدَ السِّيَرُ الْكَبِيرُ فَهُوَ الْمُعْتَمَدُ. وَيَجْمَعُ السِّتَّ كِتَابُ الْكَافِي لِلْحَاكِمِ الشَّهِيدِ، فَهُوَ الْكَافِي. أَقْوَى شُرُوحِهِ الَّذِي كَالشَّمْسِ مَبْسُوطُ شَمْسِ الْأُمَّةِ السَّرَخْسِيِّ، مُعْتَمَدُ النُّقُولِ، لَيْسَ يُعْمَلُ بِخُلْفِهِ، وَلَيْسَ عَنْهُ يُعْدَلُ.
And al-Mabsut became famous as 'al-Asl', and this is due to its preceding the six in composition. Similarly, al-Jami' al-Saghir is after it, so what is in it (al-Jami' al-Saghir) takes precedence over al-Asl, because the latter preceded. And the last of the six in composition is al-Siyar al-Kabir, so it is the reliable one. And the book al-Kafi by al-Hakim al-Shahid encompasses the six, so it is sufficient. The strongest of its commentaries, which is like the sun, is al-Mabsut of Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi, reliable in transmissions; no action is taken contrary to it, and no deviation from it is made.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage from Naseem al-Mufti lays down a rasm al-mufti rule: the hierarchy and authority among the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani. It states that al-Mabsut (also known as al-Asl) was composed first, then al-Jami' al-Saghir, and finally al-Siyar al-Kabir. The general principle is that when there is a conflict, the later composition is mu'tamad (relied upon), because it represents Imam Muhammad's final view. Thus al-Jami' al-Saghir takes precedence over al-Asl, and al-Siyar al-Kabir is the most authoritative of the six. The passage then highlights al-Kafi by al-Hakim al-Shahid as a comprehensive collection of the six books, and al-Sarakhsi's al-Mabsut as the strongest commentary—so strong that it is treated as the final authority; no fatwa is given against it, and it cannot be abandoned. This establishes a clear hierarchy for the mufti: rely on later compositions of Imam Muhammad, and on al-Sarakhsi's Mabsut as the definitive commentary.

Key Terms
المبسوط (al-Mabsut): The foundational work by Imam Muhammad, also called al-Asl; composed first among the six books.
الأصل (al-Asl): Another name for al-Mabsut by Imam Muhammad, meaning 'the foundation' because it was composed first.
الجامع الصغير (al-Jami' al-Saghir): The second of the six books in composition; its rulings override those of al-Asl.
السير الكبير (al-Siyar al-Kabir): The last of the six books in composition; considered the most reliable (mu'tamad) among them.
الكافي (al-Kafi): A book by al-Hakim al-Shahid that compiles the contents of the six books.
مبسوط شمس الأئمة السرخسي (Mabsut Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi): The commentary by al-Sarakhsi on al-Kafi; considered the most authoritative transmission and no contrary fatwa is acted upon.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) al-Mabsut (al-Asl)

C) al-Siyar al-Kabir
D) al-Kafi

Key Takeaway
The later composition among the six books takes precedence, and al-Sarakhsi's Mabsut is the highest authority for fatwa.

Hierarchy of Hanafi Texts and Imams for Fatwa
Establishes the authoritative order of sources and mujtahid imams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the hierarchy of Hanafi texts and imams for fatwa and apply it to determine the authoritative source.

Core Idea
The mufti follows a strict hierarchy: first Zahir al-Riwayah, then al-Mabsut, then imams: Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad.

A mufti must know which sources to rely on. This passage lays out the definitive hierarchy of texts and mujtahid imams within the Hanafi school. Understanding this order ensures fatwa are based on the most authoritative transmissions, preserving the integrity of the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the textual hierarchy
Note the chronological order of Imam Muhammad's six books: al-Asl (al-Mabsut) first, then al-Jami' al-Saghir, then al-Siyar al-Kabir. The later composition takes precedence in fatwa.
al-Siyar al-Kabir is the last and most relied upon.
2
Recognize the role of al-Kafi and al-Mabsut
al-Kafi compiles the six books. Its strongest commentary is al-Mabsut by al-Sarakhsi, which is the final authority.
No fatwa should contradict al-Mabsut.
3
Understand the imam hierarchy
All valid Hanafi rulings originate from Abu Hanifa's selections. If no explicit choice from him exists, the order of preference is: Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar and Hasan ibn Ziyad.
The companions swore that no other imam has independent authority.
4
Apply the hierarchy in practice
When issuing a fatwa, first check Zahir al-Riwayah texts, then al-Mabsut, then follow the imam hierarchy.

Source Text

من فِى حَلْ المعنى في حل صاحب شن عقود رسم المفى ا [؟] کتابوں میں مقدم ہے، اس کے بعد جامع صغیر ہے، لہذا جو مسائل اس میں ہیں وہ الاصل پر مقدم ہونگے ، اور آخری تصنیف السیر الکبیر ہے پس یہ معتمد کتاب ہے۔ اور مشتہر ہوئی ہے مبسوط اصل کہ کر تصنیف تھی یہ پہلی باقی رہیں مؤخر جامع صغیر کا ہے پھر اس کے بعد نمبر ترجیح میں مقدم تصنیف میں مؤخر سیر کبیر ان کی تصنیف آخری ہے ہے اعتماد اس پر تضعیف سے بری ہے وَيَجْمَعُ السِّنَّ كِتَابُ الْكَافِيُّ لِلْحَاكِمِ الشَّهِيدِ، فَهُوَ الْكَافِى أَقْوى شُرُوحِهِ الَّذِى كَالشَّمْسِ مَبْسُوطُ شَمْسِ الْأُمَّةِ السَّرَخُسِي مُعْتَمَدُ النُّقُولِ، لَيْسَ يُعْمَل بِخُلْفِهِ، وَلَيْسَ عَنْهُ يُعْدَلُ ترجمہ: اور ان چھ کتابوں کو حاکم شہید کی کتاب الکافی جمع کرتی ہے، پس یہ کتاب کافی ہے، کافی کی سب سے قوی شرح جو سورج کی طرح ہے وہ شمس الائمہ سرحسی کی مبسوط ہے، مسائل نقل کرنے میں یہ کتاب قابل اعتماد ہے، اس کے خلاف قول پر عمل نہیں کیا جائے گا اور نہ ہی اس سے عدول کیا جائے گا۔ ان چھ کتب کی جامع حاکم کی ایک کافی پہر عمل ہے کافی بہر جبل شافی ہے ہے شرح شمس امت علامہ سرخسی کی سورج کی روشنی کو جو کر گئی ہے پھیکی [؟] يم المفتى فى حَلْ مَن ۲۹ شي عقود رسم المفر ہے نقل اس کی معتمد ہے اور واجب العمل جس سے عدول کرنا ممنوع و بے محل ہے وَاعْلَمُ بِأَنَّ عَنْ أَبِي حَنِيفَةُ جَاءَتْ رِوَايَاتٌ غَدَتْ مُنِيْفَةٌ اِخْتَارَ مِنْهَا بَعْضَهَا وَالْبَاقِي يَخْتَارُ مِنْهُ سَائِرُ الرِّفَاقِ فَلَمْ يَكُنْ لِغَيْرِهِ جَوَابُ كَمَا عَلَيْهِ أَقْسَمَ الْأَصْحَابُ ترجمہ: اور جان لو! کہ امام ابو حنیفہ سے ایسی روایتیں منقول ہیں جو واضح ہیں، ان میں سے بعض کو آپ نے اختیار کر لیا اور بقیہ کو آپ کے دیگر اصحاب ( تلاندہ ) اختیار کرتے ہیں، پس (فقہ حنفی میں ) آپ کے علاوہ کسی کا کوئی قول نہیں ہے، جیسا کہ آپ کے شاگردوں نے قسم کھا کر یہ بات کہی ہے۔ اب یہ بھی جان لیجئے بے شک ہے جو حقیقت اقوال بوحنیفہ منقول ہیں بکثرت پھر منتخب کچھ ان کے قول امام ٹھہرے اور باقی ہیں منسوب دوسروں سے ہر قول ہے انھیں کا شرکت نہیں کسی کی ان کے تلامذہ نے اس پر قسم بھی کھالی وَحَيْثُ لَمْ يُوْجَدْ لَهُ اخْتِيَارُ فَقَوْلُ يَعْقُوبَ هُوَ الْمُخْتَارُ ثُمَّ محمَّدٍ، فَقَوْلُهُ الْحَسَنُ ثُمَّ زُفَرَ ، وَابْنِ زِيَادِ الْحَسَنُ ترجمہ: اور جب کسی مسئلہ میں امام صاحب کا کوئی قول موجود نہ ہو، تو امام یعقوب یعنی امام ابو یوسف کا قول مختار ہوگا ، پھر امام محمد کا قول مختار ہوگا ، پھر امام زفر اور حسن بن زیاد کا
Whoever is in the meaning of the solution, the owner of 'Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti'... [؟] The books are given precedence; after that is al-Jami' al-Saghir, so the issues in it take precedence over al-Asl. The last composition is al-Siyar al-Kabir, so it is the relied-upon book. And al-Mabsut became famous as the original composition; it was the first, the others remained later. al-Jami' al-Saghir comes first, then after it the number in priority: the one earlier in composition is later in precedence. al-Siyar al-Kabir is his last composition; reliance upon it is free from weakness. And al-Kafi by al-Hakim al-Shahid collects the six books, so it is sufficient. Its strongest commentary, which is like the sun, is al-Mabsut by Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi; it is reliable in transmission; one does not act contrary to it, nor is one allowed to deviate from it. Translation: The Kafi of Hakim Shahid collects the six books, so it is sufficient. The strongest commentary of al-Kafi, which is like the sun, is al-Mabsut by Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi. It is reliable in transmitting issues; one does not act against it, nor is one allowed to deviate from it. And know! From Abu Hanifa there have come clear narrations; he selected some of them, and the rest his other companions select. So there is no response for anyone else; as the companions swore to this. Translation: And know that from Imam Abu Hanifa many clear narrations are transmitted; some of them he himself chose, and the remaining ones his other companions choose. So in Hanafi fiqh, there is no opinion besides his, just as his students swore. And when there is no choice (explicit selection) from him, then the saying of Ya'qub (Abu Yusuf) is the chosen one, then Muhammad, then his saying is good, then Zufar and Ibn Ziyad's saying is good. Translation: And when there is no opinion of Imam Abu Hanifa in a matter, then the saying of Imam Ya'qub (Abu Yusuf) is the preferred one, then Imam Muhammad's saying, then Imam Zufar and Hasan ibn Ziyad's.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti statement establishing the hierarchy of authoritative sources within the Hanafi madhhab. It begins by explaining the chronological order of Imam Muhammad's six major works (Zahir al-Riwayah): al-Asl (al-Mabsut) was written first, then al-Jami' al-Saghir, and finally al-Siyar al-Kabir. Despite al-Asl being first, al-Jami' al-Saghir takes precedence over it in fatwa because it is later and more refined; similarly, al-Siyar al-Kabir is the last and thus the most relied-upon. Then al-Hakim al-Shahid compiled these six books into al-Kafi. The strongest commentary of al-Kafi is al-Mabsut by Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi, which is the definitive reference. The text states that one must not act against it or deviate from it. Next, the passage turns to the hierarchy of mujtahid imams. All valid Hanafi rulings ultimately trace back to Imam Abu Hanifa; his students did not create independent opinions but selected from his transmissions. This is emphasised by the fact that the companions (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, etc.) swore to this principle. When no explicit choice from Abu Hanifa exists for a given issue, the hierarchy is: first Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad al-Shaybani, then Zufar and Hasan ibn Ziyad. This rule is fundamental for muftis when they encounter a case not directly addressed by the founding imam.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Zāhir al-Riwāyah): The six books of Imam Muhammad (al-Asl, al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, al-Siyar al-Kabir, al-Ziyadat) which form the primary transmitted corpus of Hanafi fiqh.
الكافي (al-Kāfī): A book by al-Hakim al-Shahid that compiles the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah into one work.
المبسوط (al-Mabsūṭ): The commentary by Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi on al-Kafi, considered the strongest and most reliable Hanafi text for transmitting rulings.
أبو حنيفة (Abū Ḥanīfah): The eponymous founder of the Hanafi school; his personal selections (ikhtiyar) are the primary source of law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani
B) Imam Abu Yusuf
C) Imam Zufar
D) Hasan ibn Ziyad

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufti must follow a strict hierarchy: texts first (Zahir al-Riwayah, then al-Mabsut), then imams (Abu Hanifa, then Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad).

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
6 min
Hierarchy of Authority in Hanafi Fatwa: Imam, Sahibayn, and Later Scholars
Defines the hierarchy of preference among Hanafi imams' statements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi hierarchy of authority (Imam, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, then later scholars) to determine the mufta bihi ruling in a given case.

Core Idea
Follow Imam's choice first, then Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then the detailed tarjih of later scholars.

When issuing a fatwa in the Hanafi school, it is essential to know which opinion carries authority. This lesson parses a key passage from Naseem al-Mufti that establishes the hierarchy: Imam Abu Hanifa's own preference (ikhtiyar) is paramount; if absent, we follow Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar and Hasan ibn Ziyad. For cases where the Imam opposes the Sahibayn, a non-mujtahid mufti must rely on the detailed preferences (tafsil) recorded by later scholars, not on personal ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary authority
Locate the first principle: when Imam Abu Hanifa's own choice (ikhtiyar) is known, it must be followed. This is the highest authority.
The text says 'وَاعْلَمْ بِأَنَّ عَنْ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ ... اخْتَارَ مِنْهَا بَعْضَهَا'.
2
Recognize the secondary hierarchy in absence of Imam's choice
If the Imam's choice is not found, the mufti follows the hierarchy: first Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad al-Shaybani, then Zufar and Hasan ibn Ziyad.
The verse: 'وَحَيْثُ لَمْ يُوْجَدْ لَهُ اخْتِيَارُ فَقَوْلُ يَعْقُوبَ هُوَ الْمُخْتَارُ'.
3
Understand the ikhtilaf when Imam and Sahibayn differ
When Imam Abu Hanifa's opinion contradicts Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, earlier scholars differed: some gave the mufti choice, others preferred stronger evidence. These views apply only to a mujtahid mufti.
The text mentions 'وَقِيلَ بِالتَّخْيِيرِ' and 'وَقِيلَ مَنْ دَلِيلُهُ أَقْوَى رَجَحُ'.
4
Apply the rule for the present age
For a non-mujtahid mufti today, the authoritative method is 'التفصيل' – following the specific tarjihs made by later Hanafi scholars (e.g., in seventeen issues Zufar's opinion was preferred).
The verse: 'فَالْآنَ لَا تَرْجِيْحَ بِالدَّلِيلِ فَلَيْسَ إِلَّا الْقَوْلُ بِالتَّفْصِيل'.
5
Consult the works of later authorities
The mufti must refer to the compilations of the mutakhkhirin (e.g., al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya, al-Hidayah) where the detailed preferences are recorded, rather than attempting independent tarjih.
The conclusion: 'فَنَأْخُذُ الَّذِي لَهُمْ قَدْ وَضَحَا'.

Source Text

وَاعْلَمْ بِأَنَّ عَنْ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ ٢٣ جَاءَتْ رِوَايَاتٌ غَدَتْ مُنِيْفَةٌ اِخْتَارَ مِنْهَا بَعْضَهَا وَالْبَاقِي ٢٤ يَخْتَارُ مِنْهُ سَائِرُ الرِّفَاقِ فَلَمْ يَكُنْ لِغَيْرِهِ جَوَابُ ٢٥ كَمَا عَلَيْهِ أَقْسَمَ الْأَصْحَابُ ترجمہ: اور جان لو! کہ امام ابو حنیفہ سے ایسی روایتیں منقول ہیں جو واضح ہیں، ان میں سے بعض کو آپ نے اختیار کر لیا اور بقیہ کو آپ کے دیگر اصحاب ( تلاندہ ) اختیار کرتے ہیں، پس (فقہ حنفی میں ) آپ کے علاوہ کسی کا کوئی قول نہیں ہے، جیسا کہ آپ کے شاگردوں نے قسم کھا کر یہ بات کہی ہے۔
اب یہ بھی جان لیجئے بے شک ہے جو حقیقت اقوال بوحنیفہ منقول ہیں بکثرت
پھر منتخب کچھ ان کے قول امام ٹھہرے اور باقی ہیں منسوب دوسروں سے
ہر قول ہے انھیں کا شرکت نہیں کسی کی ان کے تلامذہ نے اس پر قسم بھی کھالی
وَحَيْثُ لَمْ يُوْجَدْ لَهُ اخْتِيَارُ ٢٦ فَقَوْلُ يَعْقُوبَ هُوَ الْمُخْتَارُ ٣٦ ثُمَّ محمَّدٍ، فَقَوْلُهُ الْحَسَنُ ٢٧ ثُمَّ زُفَرَ ، وَابْنِ زِيَادِ الْحَسَنُ ترجمہ: اور جب کسی مسئلہ میں امام صاحب کا کوئی قول موجود نہ ہو، تو امام یعقوب یعنی امام ابو یوسف کا قول مختار ہوگا ، پھر امام محمد کا قول مختار ہوگا ، پھر امام زفر اور حسن بن زیاد کا قول مختار ہوگا۔
قول امام اعظم موجود جب نہ پائیں یعقوب کہہ گئے جو مفتی بہ بنائیں
اور ان کے بعد نمبر ابن حسن کا رکھیں آخر میں پھر زفر ہیں ابن زیاد بھی ہیں
وَقِيلَ بِالتَّخْيِيرِ فِى فَتَوَاهُ ٢٨ إِن خَالَفَ الإمامَ صَاحِبَاهُ وَقِيلَ مَنْ دَلِيلُهُ أَقْوَى رَجَحُ ٢٩ وَذَا لِمُفْتِ ذِي اجْتِهَادِ الْأَصَحُ فَالْآنَ لَا تَرْجِيْحَ بِالدَّلِيلِ ٣٠ فَلَيْسَ إِلَّا الْقَوْلُ بِالتَّفْصِيل مَا لَمْ يَكُنُ خِلافُهُ الْمُصَحْحَا فَنَأْخُذُ الَّذِي لَهُمْ قَدْ وَضَحَا ٣١ فَإِنَّنَا نَرَاهُمُرُ قَدْ رَبَّحُوا ٣٢ مَقَالَ بَعْضٍ صَحْبِهِ وَصَحْحُوا مِنْ ذَاكَ مَا قَدْ رَبَّحُوا لِزُفَرُ ٣٣ مَقَالَهُ فِي سَبْعَةٍ وَّعَشَرُ ترجمہ: اور کہا گیا ہے کہ مفتی کو فتوی دینے میں اختیار ہوگا جب امام اور صاحبین کا اختلاف ہو، اور ایک قول یہ ہے کہ جس کی دلیل قوی ہوگی وہی قول رائج ہوگا، اور یہ قول مجتہد مفتی کے لئے زیادہ صحیح ہے، پس اب دلیل کی بناء پر ترجیح کی بات نہیں رہی، بلکہ تفصیل والا قول ہی درست ہے، جب تک کہ امام صاحب کے علاوہ قول کی تصیح نہ کی گئی ہو، تو ہم اس قول کو لے لیں گے جو اصحاب الترجیح کے لئے واضح ہوا ہے، کیوں کہ ہم انھیں دیکھتے ہیں کہ انھوں نے امام صاحب کے بعض شاگردوں کے قول کو راجح قرار دیا ہے، اسی میں سے ہے جو انھوں نے سترہ مسائل میں امام زفر کے قول کو راجح قرار دیا ہے۔ ترتیب اس طرح سے منقول ہے کتب میں قیل کے ساتھ لیکن دو قول اور بھی ہیں
And know that from Abu Hanifa many clear narrations have come. He chose some of them, and the rest are chosen by the other companions. Therefore there is no statement for anyone other than him, as the companions swore. And when his (Abu Hanifa's) choice is not found, then the statement of Ya'qub (Abu Yusuf) is the preferred one, then Muhammad's (Muhammad al-Shaybani), then Zufar and Ibn Ziyad. And it has been said that the mufti has choice in his fatawa when the two companions oppose the Imam; and it has been said that whoever's evidence is stronger is preferred. This is more correct for a mufti who is a mujtahid. But now there is no preference by evidence, so there is only the detailed method, as long as his (Imam's) contrary has not been corrected. So we take what has become clear for them. For we see that they have given tarjih to the statement of some of his companions and corrected it. From that is that they have given tarjih to Zufar's statement in seventeen issues.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti rule establishing the hierarchy of authority in the Hanafi school for issuing fatwas. The author explains that the only authoritative statements in the school are those of Imam Abu Hanifa and his students (particularly Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar, and Hasan b. Ziyad). When the Imam's own preference (اختيار) is known, it must be followed. If his choice is not found, then we follow the choice of his companions in order: first Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar and Hasan b. Ziyad. The passage then addresses a disagreement: when Imam Abu Hanifa's opinion differs from that of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), what should the mufti do? Two opinions are mentioned: (1) the mufti has choice (تخيير), and (2) preference based on stronger evidence. The author notes that the latter is more correct for a mujtahid mufti, but since the era of ijtihad has passed, now we cannot do evidence-based tarjih; instead we follow the detailed method (تفصيل) that later scholars have worked out. They have preferred opinions of certain students in specific issues, e.g., Zufar's opinion in seventeen issues. Thus, the mufti of today must rely on the works of the later authorities (mutakhkhirin) rather than direct ijtihad.

Key Terms
اختيار (ikhtiyār): The specific opinion chosen by Imam Abu Hanifa himself among multiple narrations from him.
المختار (al-mukhtār): The preferred opinion that should be followed when the Imam's choice is unknown.
التفصيل (al-tafṣīl): The detailed method of following the specific tarjihs made by later Hanafi scholars for individual issues.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what should a mufti do when no choice from Imam Abu Hanifa is found?
A) Follow the opinion of Imam Shafi'i
B) Follow Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar and Hasan b. Ziyad
C) Choose any opinion he wishes
D) Follow the majority of jurists

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi fatwa hierarchy requires following Imam's choice first, then Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, and finally later scholars' detailed preferences (tafsil).

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

The Mufti's Hierarchy When Imam and Disciples Differ
Explaining the mufti's options when Imam and Sahibayn differ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for a mufti when Imam Abu Hanifa and his disciples differ, by identifying the mufti's authority level and the source of the authenticated opinion.

Core Idea
A non-mujtahid mufti must follow authenticated opinions, not his own reasoning or free choice.

When Imam Abu Hanifa and his two major disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, disagree on a ruling, the later Hanafi school established a clear method for the mufti. This lesson unpacks three opinions recorded in the literature and explains why the third—relying on the authentication (tas-hih) of later scholars—is the operative rule for today's mufti.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three opinions
Read the Arabic/Urdu passage and separate the three views: (1) free choice (takhyir), (2) preference by evidence (tarjih bi al-dalil) for mujtahids, (3) following the authenticated opinions (musahhah) for non-mujtahids.
The text uses 'قيل' (it was said) for the first two; the third is presented as the relied-upon view.
2
Determine the mufti's level
Classify the mufti in question: is he a mujtahid or a muqallid? The passage states that tarjih bi al-dalil is for 'mufti of ijtihad', while the later part addresses non-mujtahid muftis ('now muftis are transmitters').
3
Apply the rule for non-mujtahid mufti
When the mufti is not a mujtahid, he must follow the opinion that has been authenticated (musahhah) by the ashab al-tarjih (the preferring scholars). This may be the Imam's own view or, if the Imam's view is not authenticated, a disciple's view that was preferred.
The text gives an example: Zufar's opinion was preferred in 17 issues.
4
Check the hierarchy
Note the order of precedence: first look for an authenticated ruling from Imam Abu Hanifa; if not found, then follow what the later scholars authenticated; do not resort to personal evidence-weighing.
5
Evaluate the role of later scholars
Understand that the process of tarjih is performed by specialized later scholars, not by the common mufti. The mufti's duty is to transmit their consensus.
This preserves consistency across fatwas.
