ترجمہ: اور کہا گیا ہے کہ مفتی کو فتوی دینے میں اختیار ہوگا جب امام اور صاحبین کا اختلاف ہو، اور ایک قول یہ ہے کہ جس کی دلیل قوی ہوگی وہی قول رائج ہوگا، اور یہ قول مجتہد مفتی کے لئے زیادہ صحیح ہے، پس اب دلیل کی بناء پر ترجیح کی بات نہیں رہی، بلکہ تفصیل والا قول ہی درست ہے، جب تک کہ امام صاحب کے علاوہ قول کی تصیح نہ کی گئی ہو، تو ہم اس قول کو لے لیں گے جو اصحاب الترجیح کے لئے واضح ہوا ہے، کیوں کہ ہم انھیں دیکھتے ہیں کہ انھوں نے امام صاحب کے بعض شاگردوں کے قول کو راجح قرار دیا ہے، اسی میں سے ہے جو انھوں نے سترہ مسائل میں امام زفر کے قول کو راجح قرار دیا ہے۔ ترتیب اس طرح سے منقول ہے کتب میں قیل کے ساتھ لیکن دو قول اور بھی ہیں

[Title] Naseem al-Mufti fi Hall Maba. The chosen saying is: when the saying of the great Imam is not found, Ya'qub's saying is taken as the mufta bihi. [Then] Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti. And after them, place the number of Ibn Hasan, then at the end Zufar, and Ibn Ziyad as well. 'And it was said: he has choice in his fatwa (28) when the two disciples oppose the Imam; and it was said: whoever has stronger evidence is preferred (29); and this is for a mufti who has ijtihad. The more correct is that now there is no preference by evidence (30); there is only the detailed statement that unless his (Imam's) opposite has been authenticated, we take that which has become clear to them (the scholars of tarjih) (31). For we see that they have given preponderance to the saying of some of his (Imam's) companions and authenticated from that what they have given preponderance to—for Zufar (32), his saying in seventeen issues (33).' Translation: It was said that the mufti will have choice in giving fatwa when the Imam and the two disciples differ. And another saying is that the one whose evidence is stronger will be the prevalent saying. This saying is more correct for a mujtahid mufti. Now there is no preference by evidence; rather the detailed saying is correct unless the opposite of the Imam's saying has been authenticated. Then we will take that which has become clear to the people of tarjih. Because we see that they have given preponderance to the saying of some of the Imam's students, and from that they have given preponderance to Imam Zufar's saying in seventeen issues. The arrangement is thus transmitted in books with 'qila' (it was said), but there are also two other sayings. One says that the mufti may choose whatever he wishes when the Imam is alone: he can give fatwa based on the Imam's saying or on the disciples' saying. And the second saying is that the mufti should look at the evidence and adopt the stronger saying—not every mufti can do this; it is a decision that a mujtahid will make. Muftis now are transmitters, not mujtahids. They do not look at evidences now as the detailed discussion has passed. When they find the Imam's saying authenticated, they must adopt it. If the opposite is authenticated, then they say the fatwa is on that. Preference is the right of those who keep watch over the students; they give preponderance. There are seventeen issues where Zufar is the proponent; all these issues are stronger by evidence. Those who have the right of preference will give it; we are all muqallids, we only follow.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage discusses the methodological rule (rasm al-mufti) for a Hanafi mufti when faced with a difference of opinion between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two major disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). The passage presents three views recorded in the literature. The first view grants the mufti full discretion (takhyir) to choose either opinion. The second view holds that the mufti must weigh the evidence and adopt the stronger opinion (tarjih bi al-dalil), but this is only feasible for a mujtahid mufti. The third and relied-upon view is that a non-mujtahid mufti should follow the opinions that have been authenticated (musahhah) by the later Hanafi scholars who specialized in tarjih (ashab al-tarjih). The passage explains that the practice of later scholars was to authenticate certain opinions over others, for example in seventeen issues the opinion of Imam Zufar was preferred despite being a less senior disciple. The rule thus establishes a clear hierarchy: first look for an authenticated ruling from Imam Abu Hanifa; if none, then follow what the later scholars have authenticated; the mufti must not resort to personal reasoning or free choice.

Key Terms
مُفْتِي (mufti): A scholar qualified to issue legal verdicts (fatawa). Here specifically a Hanafi mufti who is not a mujtahid but a muqallid (follower) of the school.
صَاحِبَانِ (sahibayn): The two major disciples of Imam Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
مُصَحَّح (musahhah): An opinion that has been authenticated or preferred by later Hanafi scholars (ashab al-tarjih).
تَرْجِيح (tarjih): The act of preferring one legal opinion over another based on evidence or authority. Here, the authority of later scholars who performed tarjih.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what should a non-mujtahid mufti do when Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples differ on a ruling?
A) Choose whichever he prefers.
B) Follow the opinion with the strongest evidence.
C) Follow the opinion that has been authenticated by later scholars.
D) Follow the opinion of the majority of his teachers.

Key Takeaway
A Hanafi mufti who is not a mujtahid must base his fatwa on the authenticated opinions (musahhah) within the school, not on personal preference or evidence weighing.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
8 min
Hierarchy of Fatwa for the Non-Mujtahid Mufti
Methodological rule for fatwa selection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi fatwa hierarchy to determine the source of ruling and construct a fatwa when multiple opinions exist within the school.

Core Idea
A non-mujtahid mufti must follow the verified opinion, then the majority of later authorities, never his own preference.

This passage from 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti outlines the strict hierarchy a mufti must follow when issuing fatwa. It distinguishes between the absolute mujtahid and the muqallid mufti, providing clear rules for preferring one opinion over another. The goal is to anchor fatwa in the transmitted corpus while allowing for later scholarly consensus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of mufti
Determine whether the mufti is a mujtahid (absolute or within the school) or a muqallid (transmitter). The rules differ significantly: the mujtahid may select based on evidence; the muqallid must follow the transmitted hierarchy.
The passage states that most current muftis are merely transmitters (nāqil) and not mujtahids.
2
Check for a direct riwaya (narration) from early Hanafi imams
If a riwaya from Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, or Muhammad (the Zahir al-Riwayah) exists, it is binding and must be followed. The mufti has no discretion.
Riwaya refers specifically to the six books of Imam Muhammad.
3
If no riwaya, consult the opinions of later authoritative scholars
If there is no direct transmission, the mufti looks at the rulings of later Hanafi authorities. He must give preference to the majority opinion among the specified scholars like al-Tahawi, Abu Hafs al-Kabir, Abu Ja'far al-Hinduwani, and Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi.
This is the rule: يُرَبَّحُ الَّذِي عَلَيْهِ الْأَكْثَرُ (the opinion of the majority is preferred).
4
If no opinion from those authorities, exert caution and ijtihad with fear of Allah
When none of the specified later scholars have a known saying, the mufti must study the issue with great care (jidd wa ijtihad) and fear Allah's punishment. He may not simply give any ruling but must derive from principles.
The passage warns that only a wretched person dares to speak about rulings without proper authority.
5
Apply the rule of tarjih (preference) correctly
In all cases, the mufti must follow the hierarchy: riwaya → majority of later mashayikh → careful ijtihad. The right of tarjih belongs to the earlier authorities; the muqallid merely follows.
This prevents arbitrary selection and preserves the school's integrity.

اما
ثُمَّ إِذَا لَمْ تُوجَدِ الرِّوَايَة عَنْ عُلَمَائِنَا ذَوِى الدِّرَايَهُ وَاخْتَلَفَ الَّذِينَ قَدْ تَأَخَّرُوا يُرَبَّحُ الَّذِى عَلَيْهِ الْأَكْثَرُ مِثْلُ الطَّحَاوِي وَأَبِى حَفْصِ الْكَبِير وَأَبَوَى جَعْفَرٍ وَاللَّيْث الشَّهِيرُ وَحَيْثُ لَمْ تُوجَدُ لِهؤلاءِ مَقَالَةٌ، وَاحْتِيجَ لِلإفْتَاءُ فَلْيَنظُرِ الْمُفْتِى بِجِدٌ وَاجْتِهَادُ وَلْيَخْشَ بَطْشَ رَبِّهِ يَوْمَ الْمَعَادُ فَلَيْسَ يَجُسُرُ عَلَى الْأحْكَامِ سِوَى شَقِيٌّ خَاسِرِ الْمَرَامِ ترجمہ: پھر جب کوئی روایت موجود نہ ہو ہمارے فہم و بصیرت رکھنے والے علماء سے اور بعد کے فقہاء میں اختلاف ہو جائے تو اس قول کو ترجیح دی جائے گی جو اکثر فقہاء مثلاً امام طحاوی، ابو حفص كبير ، ابو جعفر ہندوانی ، اور مشہور ابواللیث سمرقندی جیسے حضرات کا ہو، اور جہاں ان حضرات کا کوئی قول موجود نہ ہو اور فتوی دینے کی ضرورت ہو تو مفتی پوری کوشش اور محنت سے غور و فکر کرے اور چاہئے کہ پرودرگار کی قیامت کے دن کی پکڑ سے ڈرے، اس لئے کہ احکام شرعیہ کو بیان کرنے میں وہی شخص دلیری کرتا ہے جو بد بخت اور مقصد میں نا کام آدمی ہوتا ہے۔ پھر ہم اگر نہ پائیں کوئی روایت ایسی
جو صاحب درایت اعلام سے ہو مروی
ہو بعد کے مشائخ میں اختلاف پیدا اکثر کے قول پر تب مبنی رہے گا فتوی
The mufti in resolution from the commentary of 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti. There is one [type] where the mufti may give fatwa according to whatever he wishes when he is an absolute imam: he can give fatwa on the saying of an imam or on the saying of the Sahibayn. But the other [type] is that the mufti looks at the evidence, and whichever saying is stronger he adopts. Not every mufti can do this; that decision is only for a mujtahid. Now muftis are merely transmitters, not mujtahids. They do not look at detailed evidence as before. They must follow the saying of the imam as long as they find it verified (musahhah). When a correction occurs against it, then they say: the fatwa is better on this. The right of preference (tarjih) belongs to those who kept watch over the students; they have given preference. There are seventeen issues in which Zufar is the proponent; they are stronger with evidence, all those issues. The right of preference belongs to them; they will give preference. We are all muqallids; we will merely follow. Now the mufti in resolution from the commentary on 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti. The student here has given preference to them. They did whatever they had to do and left. Now most muftis are devoid of this qualification; it suffices them to simply transmit the fatwa. Then when a narration (riwaya) from our scholars of understanding (dhawu al-dirayah) is not found, and those who came later differ, the one is given preference which is the majority, such as [the opinion of] al-Tahawi, Abu Hafs al-Kabir, Abu Ja'far [al-Hinduwani], and al-Layth al-Shahir. And when no statement is found from these, and it is necessary to give fatwa, then the mufti should look with seriousness and ijtihad, and he should fear the seizure of his Lord on the Day of Return, for no one dares to [speak about] the rulings except a wretched one who fails in his goal. Translation: Then when no narration is found from our scholars of understanding, and later jurists differ, the saying which is of the majority is given preference, like Imam Tahawi, Abu Hafs al-Kabir, Abu Ja'far al-Hinduwani, and the famous Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi. And where no saying of these is found, and there is a need for fatwa, the mufti should reflect with effort and ijtihad, and he should fear the punishment of his Lord on the Day of Reckoning, because only a wretched and unsuccessful person dares to speak about Islamic rulings. Then if we do not find any such narration, which is transmitted from the scholars of understanding, and later mashayikh differ, then the fatwa will be based on the saying of the majority.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a methodological rule (rasm al-mufti) on how a mufti who is a mere transmitter (muqallid) should determine which opinion to give as fatwa. The text distinguishes two levels of mufti: the absolute mujtahid who can choose any opinion based on evidence, and the muqallid mufti who must follow the transmitted ruling. For the latter, the primary rule is to follow the saying of the Imam (Imam Abu Hanifa) as long as it has been verified (musahhah) by later scholars. If a later scholar has corrected it (by declaring another opinion stronger), then the fatwa should be given on that corrected opinion. The text then mentions a list of seventeen issues where Imam Zufar's opinion is considered stronger (aqwa) by evidence. The right of tarjih (preference) belongs to the early authorities; the muqallid simply follows. The Arabic quotation from 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti then provides the rule when no narration (riwaya) from the early Hanafi imams (like Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) is found. In that case, if later jurists differ, the majority opinion among the later authorities—specifically those renowned for their knowledge, like al-Tahawi, Abu Hafs al-Kabir (d. 216 AH), Abu Ja'far al-Hinduwani (d. 320 AH), and Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi (d. 373 AH)—is given preference. If none of these have a saying on the matter, then the mufti must exert ijtihad (but he is not a full mujtahid; it means he must carefully study and deduce from principles, but with great fear of God, as giving a fatwa without proper authority is a grave sin. This hierarchy ensures that fatwa remains anchored in the transmitted corpus while allowing flexibility for new issues.

Key Terms
الرِّوَايَةُ (al-riwayah): A transmitted narration or ruling from the early Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) that is part of the Zahir al-Riwayah corpus.
ذَوِى الدِّرَايَةِ (dhawu al-dirayah): The early Hanafi scholars who possessed deep understanding and ijtihad abilities, contrasting with mere transmitters.
الْأَكْثَرُ (al-akthar): The majority opinion among the later authoritative Hanafi jurists (e.g., Tahawi, Abu Hafs, Abu Ja'far, Abu al-Layth), used as a rule for tarjih when no early riwaya exists.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what should a mufti do when there is no narration (riwaya) from the early Hanafi imams and the later jurists differ?
A) He may choose any opinion he finds easiest.
B) He must prefer the opinion held by the majority among specified later authorities (like Tahawi and Abu al-Layth).
C) He should perform ijtihad from scratch as a full mujtahid.
D) He must follow the opinion of Abu Hanifa exclusively.

Key Takeaway
The non-mujtahid mufti must follow the verified transmission, then the majority of later Hanafi authorities, and never his own preference.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
6 min
Hierarchy When No Riwaya and Later Ikhtilaf
Rule for mufti when no classical riwaya exists and later scholars differ

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi mufti hierarchy when no early riwaya exists and later scholars differ, and identify whether to follow the majority or perform ijtihad.

Core Idea
When no early riwaya exists, the mufti follows the majority of later scholars — only then ijtihad.

A mufti often faces cases where the founding Hanafi imams left no explicit transmitted ruling. Later scholars may disagree. This rasm rule provides the next step: first, adopt the majority view among key later authorities. Only if they too are silent should the mufti engage in careful ijtihad, always mindful of divine accountability. This lesson parses that hierarchy.

Guided Path
1
Check for riwaya from early scholars
Verify if any transmitted ruling (riwaya) from Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, or Muhammad al-Shaybani exists. If it does, that rules.
If riwaya exists, no need for later opinions.
2
Identify later ikhtilaf
If no riwaya, see if later Hanafi scholars (mutakhkhirin) differ on the issue.
Key authorities include at-Tahawi, Abu Hafs al-Kabir, Abu Ja'far al-Hindwani, Abu al-Layth as-Samarqandi.
3
Apply the majority rule
Give precedence to the opinion held by the majority of those later scholars.
The Arabic says 'يُرَبَّحُ الَّذِى عَلَيْهِ الْأَكْثَرُ' – the majority is preferred.
4
If no statement from key scholars, perform ijtihad
If even the major later scholars have no recorded opinion, then the mufti must exert ijtihad with seriousness, fear of Allah, and avoid recklessness.
Only a wretched person dares to issue fatwas without this care.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ إِذَا لَمْ تُوجَدِ الرِّوَايَة عَنْ عُلَمَائِنَا ذَوِى الدِّرَايَهُ وَاخْتَلَفَ الَّذِينَ قَدْ تَأَخَّرُوا يُرَبَّحُ الَّذِى عَلَيْهِ الْأَكْثَرُ مِثْلُ الطَّحَاوِي وَأَبِى حَفْصِ الْكَبِير وَأَبَوَى جَعْفَرٍ وَاللَّيْث الشَّهِيرُ وَحَيْثُ لَمْ تُوجَدُ لِهؤلاءِ مَقَالَةٌ، وَاحْتِيجَ لِلإفْتَاءُ فَلْيَنظُرِ الْمُفْتِى بِجِدٌ وَاجْتِهَادُ وَلْيَخْشَ بَطْشَ رَبِّهِ يَوْمَ الْمَعَادُ فَلَيْسَ يَجْسُرُ عَلَى الْأحْكَامِ سِوَى شَقِيٌّ خَاسِرِ الْمَرَامِ ترجمہ: پھر جب کوئی روایت موجود نہ ہو ہمارے فہم و بصیرت رکھنے والے علماء سے اور بعد کے فقہاء میں اختلاف ہو جائے تو اس قول کو ترجیح دی جائے گی جو اکثر فقہاء مثلاً امام طحاوی، ابو حفص كبير ، ابو جعفر ہندوانی ، اور مشہور ابواللیث سمرقندی جیسے حضرات کا ہو، اور جہاں ان حضرات کا کوئی قول موجود نہ ہو اور فتوی دینے کی ضرورت ہو تو مفتی پوری کوشش اور محنت سے غور و فکر کرے اور چاہئے کہ پرودرگار کی قیامت کے دن کی پکڑ سے ڈرے، اس لئے کہ احکام شرعیہ کو بیان کرنے میں وہی شخص دلیری کرتا ہے جو بد بخت اور مقصد میں نا کام آدمی ہوتا ہے۔ پھر ہم اگر نہ پائیں کوئی روایت ایسی جو صاحب درایت اعلام سے ہو مروی ہو بعد کے مشائخ میں اختلاف پیدا اکثر کے قول پر تب مبنی رہے گا فتوی
Then, when there is no narration from our scholars of insight and the later ones differed, the one followed by the majority is given precedence, such as at-Tahawi, Abu Hafs al-Kabir, Abu Ja'far [al-Hindwani], and the famous al-Layth [as-Samarqandi]. And when there is no statement from these and there is need for fatwa, the mufti should look with seriousness and ijtihad, and fear the seizing of his Lord on the Day of Return, for only a wretched failure of purpose dares to issue rulings. Translation: Then when no narration exists from our scholars of understanding and insight, and the later jurists disagree, the saying that is given precedence is that of the majority of jurists, such as Imam at-Tahawi, Abu Hafs al-Kabir, Abu Ja'far al-Hindwani, and the famous Abu al-Layth as-Samarqandi. And where there is no saying of these and there is need for fatwa, the mufti should exert full effort and ijtihad, and fear the punishment of his Lord on the Day of Judgment, for only a wretched and failed person dares to pronounce legal rulings. Then if we find no such narration transmitted from the discerning scholars, and there is disagreement among later mashayikh, the fatwa will be based on the saying of the majority.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a rasm rule for the mufti's decision-making hierarchy. It states that if no explicit riwaya (transmitted ruling) from the early Hanafi scholars (those with deep insight) is found, and later scholars (mutakhkhirin) differ, the mufti should give precedence to the opinion of the majority among them. Key examples of such later authorities are Imam at-Tahawi, Abu Hafs al-Kabir, Abu Ja'far al-Hindwani, and Abu al-Layth as-Samarqandi. If even these scholars have no explicit statement on the issue, the mufti must perform ijtihad with utmost effort and fear of Allah, as only a wretched person would be bold in issuing fatwas without such care. The Urdu commentary reiterates this: if no riwaya from the learned scholars exists, and later mashayikh differ, the fatwa is based on the majority's position.

Key Terms
الرِّوَايَة (ar-riwaya): A transmitted ruling from the early Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad).
الْأَكْثَرُ (al-akthar): The majority among the later Hanafi jurists (mutakhkhirin).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, when no riwaya from the early Hanafi scholars exists and the later scholars differ, which opinion should the mufti prefer?
A) The opinion of the earliest scholar available
B) The opinion of the majority of later scholars
C) The opinion of Abu Hanifa alone
D) The opinion of the mufti's own teacher

Key Takeaway
In the absence of a transmitted ruling from the early imams, the mufti first seeks the majority opinion among key later scholars; only if that fails does he resort to ijtihad, always with fear of Allah.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
6 min
Two Key Rules for Mufti on Ibadat and Qada
Presents two dawabit for preferring Hanafi imams in ibadat and qada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the two Hanafi dawabit for preferring imams in ibadat (Abu Hanifa) and qada (Abu Yusuf), including the exception for dhawi al-arham inheritance (Muhammad).

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa preferred in worship, Abu Yusuf in judiciary, Muhammad in distant relatives' inheritance.

This passage from Naseem al-Mufti presents two foundational regulatory rules (dawabit) that guide the mufti in selecting which Hanafi imam's opinion to follow. These rules streamline the fatwa process, especially when multiple opinions exist. The first rule covers all acts of worship, the second covers judicial matters with a special sub-rule for distant relatives. Mastering these rules is essential for systematic fatwa practice.

Guided Path
1
State the first dabit
Identify the rule for ibadat: in all chapters of worship, the opinion of Abu Hanifa is given absolute preference, unless there is a report from him that the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) have adopted. This exception recognizes that the Sahibayn may have chosen a different position based on later deliberation.
The example given is tayammum for a person who has thrown dates into water – the Imam's view is favored.
2
State the second dabit
Identify the rule for qada: for every subsidiary matter related to judiciary, the opinion of Abu Yusuf is selected. This is due to his extensive experience as a judge. Additionally, for inheritance of distant relatives (dhawi al-arham), Muhammad's opinion is preferred.
These are two sub-rules under the broader category of judicial preference.
3
Recognize the supporting principles
The passage also affirms that istihsan is preferred over qiyas, except for a few specific matters. Moreover, the Zahir al-Riwaya (the well-known transmissions) is the standard; one should not depart from it to a conflicting report simply because that report is transmitted.
These principles reinforce the hierarchy of authority.
4
Apply the rules to a scenario
Consider a judge needing a ruling on a contract dispute: according to the second dabit, he should follow Abu Yusuf's opinion. For an inheritance case involving a distant cousin (dhawi al-arham), he should follow Muhammad's opinion.
The mufti must know which rule applies to which type of case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَهَهُنَا ضَوَابِطُ مُحَرَّرَةٌ غَدَتْ لَدَى أَهْلِ النُّهَى مُقَرَّرَةٌ فِي كُلِّ أَبْوَابِ الْعِبَادَاتِ يُرَجَّحُ قَوْلُ الإِمَامِ مُطْلَقاً مَا لَمْ تَصِحَّ لَهُ رِوَايَةٌ بِهَا الْغَيْرُ أَخَذَ مِثْلُ تَيَمُّمِ لِمَنْ تَمْرًا نَبَذَ . وَكُلُّ فَرْعٍ بِالْقَضَاءِ تَعَلَّقَا قَوْلُ أَبِي يُوسُفَ فِيهِ يُنْتَقَى وَفِي مَسَائِلِ ذَوِي الْأَرْحَامِ قَدْ أَفْتَوْا بِمَا يَقُولُهُ مُحَمَّدُ وَرَجَّحُوا اسْتِحْسَانَهُمْ عَلَى الْقِيَاسِ إِلَّا مَسَائِلَ وَمَا فِيهَا اشْتِبَاهُ ظَاهِرُ الرِّوَايَةِ لَيْسَ يُعْدَلُ عَنْهُ إِلَى خِلَافِهِ إِذْ يُنْقَلُ
And here are refined regulations that have been settled among people of intelligence: In all chapters of worship, the saying of the Imam (Abu Hanifa) is given preference absolutely, as long as there is no report from him which the other (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) have adopted, like the tayammum of one who throws dates (into water). And every branch that pertains to judiciary, the saying of Abu Yusuf is selected therein. And in the issues of distant relatives (dhawi al-arham), they have given fatwa according to what Muhammad says. And they have preferred their istihsan over qiyas except in a few matters, and there is no ambiguity in that. The apparent transmission (Zahir al-Riwaya) is not deviated from to its contrary when (another report) is transmitted.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage presents two important regulatory rules (dawabit) for the mufti. The first rule: In all chapters of worship ('ibadat), the opinion of Imam Abu Hanifa is given preference unconditionally, unless there is a report from him that contradicts that preference and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) have adopted that contradictory report. An example is the case of tayammum for a person who has thrown dates into water—this refers to the issue of using water mixed with dates for purification. The second set of rules: For any matter related to judiciary (qada), the opinion of Abu Yusuf is preferred; for inheritance of distant relatives (dhawi al-arham), the opinion of Muhammad al-Shaybani is given preference. In cases of juristic preference (istihsan) versus analogy (qiyas), istihsan is preferred over qiyas, except for a few specific issues (isolated cases). The clear and authentic transmission (Zahir al-Riwaya) is the basis; one should not depart from it to a conflicting view when that conflicting view is transmitted (i.e., the Zahir al-Riwaya is the standard unless overridden by a stronger or more specific report).

Key Terms
ضابط (dabit): A regulatory rule or maxim governing preference between Hanafi imams
عبادات (ibadat): Acts of worship (prayer, fasting, etc.)
قضاء (qada): Judicial matters including court proceedings
ذوو الأرحام (dhawi al-arham): Distant blood relatives who are not asabah or fard heirs
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwaya): The well-known, authoritative transmissions from the founding imams (six books of Muhammad)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which Imam's opinion is preferred in matters of judicial rulings?
A) Abu Hanifa
B) Abu Yusuf
C) Muhammad al-Shaybani
D) All equally

Key Takeaway
A mufti must apply different preference rules: Abu Hanifa for worship, Abu Yusuf for judiciary, and Muhammad for distant relatives' inheritance, with the Sahibayn's adoption as an exception.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
8 min
The Rasm al-Mufti: Preferring the Right Imam for the Right Case
Rules for preferring opinions in court cases and inheritance of distant relatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when to apply the preference for Abu Yusuf (in qada matters) versus Muhammad al-Shaybani (in dhawi al-arham inheritance) and explain the authority of zahir al-riwaya.

Core Idea
Choose Abu Yusuf for court, Muhammad for distant relatives; never abandon the well-known transmitted corpus.

This passage from Naseem al-Mufti provides essential guidelines for a Hanafi mufti or qadi. It specifies distinct contexts: judicial matters require the practical rulings of Abu Yusuf, while inheritance calculations for distant relatives (dhawi al-arham) follow Muhammad al-Shaybani. Moreover, it affirms the absolute authority of the zahir al-riwaya, the primary transmitted corpus of the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two distinct contexts
The passage mentions qada (judicial) and dhawi al-arham (distant relatives). Note which imam is preferred in each: Abu Yusuf for qada, Muhammad for dhawi al-arham.
2
Understand the rationale
Abu Yusuf's opinions were more practical for court proceedings; Muhammad's rulings on dhawi al-arham expanded the category of heirs via istihsan (juristic preference).
3
Recognize the rule of istihsan over qiyas
In most dhawi al-arham cases, istihsan is preferred over qiyas, except for a few known exceptions indicated by the phrase 'illa masa'il'.
4
Grasp the principle of zahir al-riwaya
The zahir al-riwaya (well-known transmitted corpus of Imam Muhammad) is authoritative and cannot be set aside by a contrary transmission. The passage clearly states: 'laysa yu'dalu 'anhu ila khilafihi idh yunqalu'.
5
Apply the rule in practice
In a court case (qada), a qadi must follow the opinion of Abu Yusuf. In calculating inheritance for distant relatives (dhawi al-arham), a mufti must use Muhammad's opinion. Both rules are part of the rasm al-mufti.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَكُلُّ فَرُعِ بِالْقَضَا تَعَلَّقَا ۴۳ قَوْلُ أَبِى يُوسُفَ فِيهِ يُنتَقَى وَفِي مَسَائِلِ ذَوِى الْأَرْحَامِ، قَدْ ۴۴ أَفْتَوُا بِمَا يَقُولُهُ مُحَمَّدُ وَرَبَّحُوا اسْتِحْسَانَهُمْ عَلَى الْقِيَاسِ ۴۵ إِلَّا مَسَائِلَ، وَمَا فِيهَا الْعِبَاسُ ظَاهِرُ الْمَرُوقٌ، لَيْسَ يُعْدَلُ ٤٦ عَنْهُ إلَى خِلَافِهِ، إِذْ يُنْقَلُ ترجمہ: اور ہر وہ جزئیہ جس کا قضاء سے تعلق ہے اس میں امام ابو یوسف کا قول مختار ہے، اور ذوی الارحام کے مسائل میں فقہاء نے امام محمد کے قول پر فتوی دیا ہے، اور فقہاء نے قیاسی مسائل پر استحسان والے قول کو راجح قرار دیا ہے سوائے چند مسائل کے، اور ان مسائل میں کوئی اشتباہ نہیں ہے، اور ظاہر الروایہ سے اس کے مخالف قول کی طرف عدول نہیں کیا جائے گا جب اس کے خلاف دوسری روایت منقول ہو۔
جن مسئلوں کا ہوگا امر قضاء سے رشتہ ان میں تو بس چلے گا یعقوب ہی کا فتوی
ارحام کی بٹے گی میراث کس طرح سے ہیں معتبر بس اس میں ابن حسن کے فتوی
ترک قیاس کر کے مستحسن ہی کو لیجئے کچھ مسئلے ہیں ایسے جن میں خلاف کیجئے
مشہور مسئلے ہیں بے التباس کہتے
معقول بھی ہیں ،
تحت القیاس کہئے
بس ظاہر الروایہ ہی لائق عمل ہے جس کے مقابلے میں ہر نقل بے دخل ہے
And every branch [case] that relates to qada, 43 the statement of Abu Yusuf in it is selected. And in the matters of dhawi al-arham, 44 they gave fatwa with what Muhammad says, and they preferred istihsan over qiyas 45 except for a few matters, and there is no confusion in them. The apparent [zahir] of the narration [al-riwaya] 46 is not to be turned away from to its contrary, when a transmission is reported. Urdu translation: And every case that is related to qada, in it the opinion of Imam Abu Yusuf is chosen. And in the matters of dhawi al-arham, the jurists gave fatwa with the opinion of Imam Muhammad. And the jurists preferred the istihsan opinion over qiyas except for a few cases. And those cases are known without confusion, and the apparent of the riwaya is not to be abandoned for its contrary when a transmission against it is reported. Those matters that are connected with qada, only Yaqub's fatwa will be followed. How the inheritance of arham is divided? In that only Ibn al-Hasan's fatwa is considered valid. Abandon qiyas, adopt mustahsan; there are some cases in which you should oppose. The well-known cases are without confusion. They are also reasonable. They are said to be under qiyas. Only the zahir al-riwaya is worthy of practice; any other transmission is excluded.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage presents several rasm rules for the mufti. First, the verse states that every subordinate case (far') that pertains to judicial matters (qada) — i.e., cases brought to a judge — the opinion of Imam Abu Yusuf is to be selected. This is because his views were often more practical and suited for court proceedings. Second, in matters of dhawi al-arham (distant relatives who are not primary heirs), the fatwa is given according to Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani. This is a recognized rule in Hanafi inheritance law: the expansion of the category of heirs through istihsan over qiyas. Third, in general, istihsan (juristic preference) is given precedence over qiyas, except for a few known exceptions. Fourth, and most importantly, the relied-upon position from the zahir al-riwaya (the well-known transmitted corpus of Imam Muhammad's books) is not to be abandoned for a contrary transmission, even if such a transmission exists. The subsequent Urdu verses reinforce these points in poetic form, emphasizing that only Abu Yusuf's fatwa is used in court cases, only Muhammad's ruling is accepted in distant relative inheritance, and that the zahir al-riwaya remains the authoritative source for fatwa.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada'): Judicial matters; cases that come before a qadi (judge)
ذوي الأرحام (dhawi al-arham): Distant relatives who are not ashab al-fara'id nor 'asaba; they inherit by way of being uterine relatives
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwaya): The well-known, transmitted corpus of rulings from the six books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a Hanafi court case regarding a debt dispute, which imam's opinion should the qadi primarily follow according to this passage?
A) Imam Abu Hanifa's opinion
B) Imam Abu Yusuf's opinion
C) Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani's opinion
D) The opinion of the later scholars

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the ruling for court cases follows Abu Yusuf; for distant relative inheritance, Muhammad al-Shaybani; and the zahir al-riwaya is the ultimate source.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
10 min
Three Fundamental Rasm al-Mufti Rules: Diraya with Riwaya, Weak Narration Negating Kufr, and Retraction as Abrogation
Three rasm al-mufti rules in poetic form with Urdu commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply three rasm al-mufti rules: prioritize a diraya supported by a riwaya, accept a weak narration that negates kufr from a Muslim, and treat a mujtahid's retraction as abrogation.

Core Idea
Do not abandon a reasoned ruling if a transmission supports it; accept weak evidence to avoid takfir; a mujtahid's retraction abrogates his earlier fatwa.

This passage from a classical Hanafi rasm al-mufti text presents three essential rules that every mufti must internalize. They govern how a mufti should weigh his own reasoning against transmitted reports, how to handle evidence that negates disbelief, and how to treat a mujtahid's change of opinion. Mastering these rules ensures that fatwas are issued with both methodological integrity and compassion toward believers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three rules
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. List the three distinct legal rules (qawaid) that the author presents. They are marked by 'و' (and) and separated by 'وكل' (and every).
The three rules are: 1) Do not abandon a diraya if a riwaya supports it; 2) Accept any saying that negates kufr even if weak; 3) A mujtahid's retraction renders his earlier opinion like abrogated.
2
Analyze Rule 1: Diraya supported by Riwaya
Understand the interplay between diraya (juristic reasoning) and riwaya (transmitted report). The rule instructs the mufti not to abandon his own reasoned conclusion if he finds a transmitted narration from the school's imams that agrees with it. This places a premium on internal consistency within the madhhab.
Diraya is not independent; it must be aligned with the school's transmitted positions.
3
Analyze Rule 2: Weak narration negating Kufr
This rule overrides normal hadith criticism. Even if a narration is weak (da'if), if it states that a particular action does not constitute kufr (disbelief), it must be accepted as the basis for fatwa. The reason is the gravity of takfir and the principle of preserving Muslim blood and honor.
This is an exception to the general rule that weak narrations are not used in establishing rulings.
4
Analyze Rule 3: Retraction as abrogation
When a mujtahid (one of the founding Hanafi imams) retracts an earlier opinion, that earlier opinion becomes like a mansukh (abrogated) ruling. The mufti must then adopt the later opinion. This reflects the dynamic nature of fiqh within the madhhab.
The retraction does not invalidate the earlier ijtihad but changes the operative fatwa.
5
Synthesize the hierarchy for fatwa
Combine the three rules into a single fatwa methodology: First, rely on diraya consistent with riwaya. Second, when facing potential takfir, prioritize even weak narrations that negate kufr. Third, always use the most recent authoritative positions from the mujtahids.
These rules form part of the mufti's internal decision-making process, not a rigid sequence.

Source Text

لا يَنبَغِى الْعُدُولُ عَنْ دِرَايَهُ ٤٧ إِذَا أَتَى بِوَفُقِهَا رِوَايَهُ وَكُلُّ قَوْلِ جَاءَ يَنْفِي الْكُفْرَا ۲۸ عَنْ مُسْلِمٍ، وَلَوْ ضَعِيفاً أَحْرَى وَكُلُّ مَا رَجَعَ عَنْهُ المُجْتَهِد و صَارَ كَمَنْسُوخ فَغَيْرُهُ اعْتُمِد ترجمہ: اور دلیل سے عدول کرنا مناسب نہیں ہے جب کہ اس کے موافق کوئی روایت بھی ہو، اور ہر ایسا قول جو مسلم سے کفر کی نفی کرتا ہوا گر چہ وہ ضعیف ہو قبول کرنے کے زیادہ لائق ہے، اور ہر وہ قول جس سے مجتہد نے رجوع کر لیا ہے وہ گویا منسوخ ہو گیا ہے، پس اس کے علاوہ قول پر اعتماد کیا جائے گا۔
معقول ہے درایت وابستہ ہے روایت اس کو نہ چھوڑیں ہر گز کہتی ہے یہ درایت
اور ایسا قول جس سے منفی ہو کفر مسلم گو وہ ضعیف تر ہو تسلیم کرلے عالم
فتوی نہ کفر کا دے روکے زباں کو اپنی جو کہہ گئے مشائخ مانے ہر ایک مفتی
فتوے سے مجتہد جب اپنے رجوع کرلے مرجوع فیصلہ کو منسوخ ہی وہ مانے
It is not appropriate to depart from a [ruling based on] diraya 47 when a riwaya comes in agreement with it. And every saying that negates kufr 28 from a Muslim, even if weak, is more worthy [of acceptance]. And every [ruling] from which the mujtahid has retracted becomes as if abrogated; so rely on other than it. Urdu translation: And it is not appropriate to depart from a ruling based on reason (diraya) when there is a transmission (riwaya) consistent with it. And every such saying that negates kufr from a Muslim, even if it is weak, is more deserving of acceptance. And every ruling from which the mujtahid has retracted, it is as if abrogated, so other than that will be relied upon. Diraya is reasonable; it is connected to riwaya; we should never abandon it; this diraya says so. And such a saying that negates the kufr of a Muslim, even if it is very weak, the scholar should accept it. Do not issue a fatwa of kufr; restrain your tongue; every mufti should follow what the masters said. When a mujtahid retracts from his fatwa, that retracted ruling is considered abrogated.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage provides three important rasm al-mufti rules. First, a mufti should not abandon a ruling that he derives through his own understanding (diraya) if there is a transmitted report (riwaya) that supports it. This means that even if a mufti arrives at a conclusion through rational reasoning, if that conclusion aligns with a narration from the founding imams, he should prefer it and not turn away from it. Second, any statement that negates the attribution of kufr (disbelief) to a Muslim should be accepted, even if the chain of transmission is weak. The principle is that it is better to err on the side of declaring a Muslim believer, and avoiding takfir (excommunication) is of paramount importance. Thus, if a weak narration asserts that a particular action does not constitute kufr, that narration is to be given preference over stronger evidence that might indicate kufr. Third, when a mujtahid (i.e., one of the founding Hanafi imams) retracts an earlier opinion, that opinion is analogous to an abrogated ruling (mansukh) and should not be relied upon. Instead, the mufti should adopt the later opinion. The subsequent Urdu verses reiterate these points, adding practical advice: the mufti should not issue a fatwa of kufr and should follow the way of the mashayikh (Hanafi masters) in this regard.

Key Terms
دراية (diraya): Rational understanding or juristic reasoning derived from evidence
رواية (riwaya): A transmitted report, specifically a narration of a ruling from a Hanafi imam
رجوع (ruju'): Retraction by a mujtahid of his earlier opinion

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what should a mufti do if he encounters a weak narration stating that a particular action does not constitute kufr?
A) Reject it because it is weak
B) Accept it because it negates kufr
C) Require stronger evidence before accepting
D) Issue a fatwa of kufr cautiously

Key Takeaway
A mufti must never abandon a diraya supported by a riwaya, must accept weak narrations that negate kufr, and must treat a mujtahid's retraction as an abrogation of the earlier opinion.

Three Fundamental Rules (Rasm al-Mufti) for the Mufti: Diraya, Negating Kufr, and Retraction
Explanation of three fundamental rasm rules for muftis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and apply the three fundamental rules of fatwa (rasm al-mufti) regarding diraya, negation of kufr, and retraction of a mujtahid.

Core Idea
A mufti must not abandon diraya when riwaya supports it, must avoid takfir even with weak evidence, and treat retracted opinions as abrogated.

The fatwa process requires more than blind transmission; the mufti must exercise reasoned judgment (diraya) but within the bounds of transmitted texts (riwaya). This passage from the classical rasm al-mufti literature lays down three cardinal rules that protect the integrity of fatwa and the faith of Muslims. These rules are not mere suggestions — they are binding principles that guide the mufti's methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three rules
List each rule explicitly from the passage: (1) Do not abandon diraya when a conforming riwaya exists. (2) Accept any statement that negates kufr from a Muslim, even if weak. (3) A retracted opinion is like abrogated; rely on the later view.
2
Analyse the first rule: diraya and riwaya
The condition for using diraya is that a riwaya conforms to it (bi-wufqiha). The rule prohibits abandoning diraya when such a riwaya exists — it does not permit diraya to override riwaya, but rather to work in harmony.
The legal definition dissector for diraya clarifies its components.
3
Analyse the second rule: negation of kufr
Any statement that prevents declaring a Muslim a disbeliever, even if its transmission is weak, should be preferred. This reflects the overarching principle of avoiding takfir.
4
Analyse the third rule: retraction
If a mujtahid (e.g., Abu Hanifa) retracts an earlier opinion, that opinion becomes like abrogated (mansukh). The mufti must rely on the later opinion.
This prevents reliance on outdated rulings.
5
Apply these rules to a scenario
Consider a mufti who finds a weak hadith that could prevent takfir of a Muslim who made a borderline statement. Which rule applies? The second rule: the weak hadith should be accepted because it negates kufr.

معقول ہے درایت وابستہ ہے روایت اس کو نہ چھوڑیں ہر گز کہتی ہے یہ درایت
اور ایسا قول جس سے منفی ہو کفر مسلم گو وہ ضعیف تر ہو تسلیم کرلے عالم
فتوی نہ کفر کا دے روکے زباں کو اپنی جو کہہ گئے مشائخ مانے ہر ایک مفتی
فتوے سے مجتہد جب اپنے رجوع کرلے مرجوع فیصلہ کو منسوخ ہی وہ مانے
It is not proper to deviate from rational understanding (diraya) when a transmission (riwaya) conforms to it. And every statement that negates disbelief (kufr) from a Muslim, even if weak, is more worthy [of acceptance]. And every statement that the mujtahid has retracted from becomes like abrogated, so rely on other than it.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage presents three rasm rules for muftis from the poem. First, a mufti must not abandon a sound rational interpretation (diraya) when there is a transmitted report (riwaya) that supports it — this prevents arbitrary rejection of transmitted texts in favor of mere opinion. Second, any statement that negates the status of disbelief (kufr) from a Muslim, even if weak in transmission, is to be preferred, because avoiding takfir is a higher objective. Third, if a mujtahid (like Abu Hanifa) has retracted an earlier opinion, that opinion is treated as abrogated (mansukh), and the mufti must rely on the later opinion. The Urdu commentary reinforces these points with didactic verses.

Key Terms
درایت (diraya): Rational understanding or juristic reasoning exercised by a mujtahid or mufti.
روایت (riwaya): A transmitted report or narration, especially from authoritative sources.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It is considered weak but still admissible

Key Takeaway
A mufti must harmonize diraya with riwaya, prioritize avoiding takfir, and follow the mujtahid's later opinions.

The Basic Hierarchy: Mutun, Shuruh, and Fatawa
Establishing the order of precedence among legal texts for fatwa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that mutun (core texts) take precedence over shuruh (commentaries) and identify when an explicit correction overrides this hierarchy.

Core Idea
Mutun are preferred over shuruh unless the shuruh explicitly correct them.

When a mufti faces conflicting statements between a core text (matn) and a commentary (sharh), the default rule is to prefer the matn. This lesson explains the rationale and the exception: if the commentary explicitly corrects the matn, then the corrected version is preferred. Understanding this hierarchy is fundamental for issuing sound fatwas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the source types
Distinguish between a matn (core text), a sharh (commentary), and a fatwa collection. Examples: al-Quduri (matn), al-Hidayah (sharh), Fatawa al-Hulwani (old fatwa collection).
The hierarchy applies across all chapters of fiqh.
2
Understand the default precedence
The passage states that every statement in the mutun has an implied tarjih (preference). Therefore, mutun are preferred over shuruh, and shuruh over old fatwa collections.
This is the default rule; the exception comes next.
3
Recognize the exception: explicit correction
If a commentary explicitly corrects a different wording from the matn (using لفظاً صححا), then the corrected version becomes the most preponderant.
The correction must be explicit (صريح), not merely a different opinion.
4
Apply to a hypothetical
Given a case where al-Quduri says X, and al-Hidayah says Y without explicit correction, prefer X. If al-Hidayah explicitly corrects al-Quduri, prefer Y.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَكُلُّ قَوْل فِي الْمُتُونِ البَنَا ۵۰ فَذَاكَ تَرْجِحْ لَهُ صِمْنَا أَنى فَرُجْحَتُ عَلَى الشَّرُوحِ وَالشُّروْحُ ۵۱ عَلَى الْفَتَاوَى الْقِدْمِ مِن ذَاتِ رُجُوحٍ مَا لَمْ يَكُنْ سِوَاهُ لَفْظاً صُحْحَا ۵۲ فَالْأَرْجَحُ الَّذِي بِهِ قَدْ صُرِّحًا
وَسَابِقُ الْأقْوَالِ فِى الْخَانِيَه ۵۳ وَمُلْتَقَى الْأَبْحُرِ ذُو مَزِيَّهُ وَفِي سِوَاهُمَا اعْتُمِدَ مَا أَخَّرُوا ۵۴ دَلِيلَهُ لأَنَّهُ المُحَرَّرُ كَمَا هُوَ الْعَادَةُ فِى الْهَدَايَه ۵۵ وَنَحْوِهَا لِرَاجِحَ الدِّرَايَهُ كَذَا إِذَا مَا وَاحِداً قَدْ عَلَّلُوا ۵۶ لَهُ، وَتَعْلِيْلَ سِوَاهُ أَهْمَلُوا
ترجمہ: اور ہر وہ قول جس کو متون میں لیا گیا ہے تو یہ اس قول کی ضمن ترجیح ہے جو اس کو حاصل ہوگئی ہے، پس ترجیح دی جائے گی متون کو شروح پر اور شروح کو قدیم فتاوی پر ، یہ ترجیح اصحاب الترجیح کی جانب سے ہے، جب تک متون کے علاوہ قول کی صراحتا صحیح نہ کی گئی ہو، ورنہ وہ قول زیادہ رائج ہوگا جس کی صراحا صحیح کی گئی ہے۔ مذکور متن ہونا تصحیح ہے مسلم
اس پہلو سے ہوئی ہیں شرحوں پہ یہ مقدم
متن اور شروح و فتوی ترتیب ان میں رکھیں متن آپ پہلے دیکھیں پھر شرح و فتوی دیکھیں
تصحیح متن کو بھی ضمنی ہی آپ جانیں جب ہو کہیں مصرح تقدیم اس کی
چاہیں
And every statement that is taken in the mutun (core texts) — that is an implied tarjih (preference) for it. So the mutun are given preference over the shuruh (commentaries), and the shuruh over the old fatawa, from the people of tarjih, unless a different wording is explicitly corrected – then the one explicitly corrected is more preponderant. And the earlier statements in al-Khaniyyah and Multaqa al-Abhar have superiority. And in other than them, rely on what they delayed (i.e., later statements) because it is the revised (muharrar) version, as is the custom in al-Hidayah and its like for the preponderant diraya. Likewise, when they gave a single ta'lil (legal reasoning) for it and neglected other ta'lils.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage details the hierarchy of sources for fatwa. First, rulings found in the mutun (authoritative primary texts like al-Mukhtasar, al-Quduri, etc.) carry implied preference over commentaries (shuruh) because they are the foundation. However, if a commentary explicitly corrects a matn statement, that correction takes precedence. Similarly, commentaries are preferred over old fatwa collections. Among fatwa collections, statements in Fatawa Qadikhan and Multaqa al-Abhar enjoy special weight because their authors were meticulous. In other works, the later-articulated opinion is preferred because it reflects the muharrar (revised, refined) position, as seen in al-Hidayah. Finally, if a single ta'lil is given and others are omitted, that indicates the preferred reasoning. This hierarchy ensures consistency and prevents arbitrary selection.

Key Terms
متون (mutun): The foundational primary texts of the Hanafi school, such as al-Mukhtasar, al-Quduri, etc.
شروح (shuruh): Commentaries on the mutun, e.g., al-Hidayah, al-Kafi.
قديم الفتاوى (qadim al-fatawa): Early fatwa collections like Fatawa al-Hulwani, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the preferred source if a matn and a commentary differ, and the commentary does not explicitly correct the matn?
A) The commentary is preferred because it is later.
B) The matn is preferred because it is the primary text.
C) Both are considered equal.
D) The fatwa collection is preferred.

Key Takeaway
The hierarchy of Hanafi fatwa is: mutun > shuruh > qadim al-fatawa, with the exception that explicit correction in a commentary overrides the matn.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Preference for Later Statements and Ta'lil
Establishing the order of precedence among legal texts for fatwa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that later statements in authoritative works are preferred as muharrar, and that a single given ta'lil indicates the preponderant reasoning.

Core Idea
Later opinions are preferred because they reflect revised positions, and a single ta'lil signals the established argument.

The second part of the passage addresses how to prioritize when multiple statements exist in fatwa collections. It gives special weight to statements in Fatawa Qadikhan (al-Khaniyyah) and Multaqa al-Abhar. For other works, the later-uttered statement (what they 'delayed') is preferred because it is the revised (muharrar) version, as seen in al-Hidayah. Additionally, if only one ta'lil is given and others are omitted, that ta'lil is the preponderant one. This lesson unpacks these rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two special works
The passage mentions 'al-Khaniyyah' (Fatawa Qadikhan) and 'Multaqa al-Abhar'. These works have inherent superiority (مزية) due to their authors' meticulous methodology.
They are given special weight even though they are earlier fatwa collections.
2
Understand the rule for other works: prefer later statements
For works other than the two special ones, the later statement (ما أخروا) is relied upon because it is the muharrar (revised) version. This is the custom in al-Hidayah and similar works.
'Later' means the author's later opinion, not necessarily a chronological order of books.
3
Recognize the ta'lil rule
When a single ta'lil (legal reasoning) is given for a ruling and other possible ta'lils are neglected, that ta'lil is the preponderant one.
This indicates that the author considered only that reasoning as valid.
4
Apply to a scenario
Given a contradiction between an earlier statement in al-Hidayah and a later statement in the same book, prefer the later one. If a fatwa collection gives only one reason for a ruling, take that as the relied-upon reasoning.
Be careful: the special works override this 'later' rule within their scope.

However, if a commentary explicitly corrects a matn statement, that correction takes precedence. Similarly, commentaries are preferred over old fatwa collections. Among fatwa collections, statements in Fatawa Qadikhan and Multaqa al-Abhar enjoy special weight because their authors were meticulous. In other works, the later-articulated opinion is preferred because it reflects the muharrar (revised, refined) position, as seen in al-Hidayah. Finally, if a single ta'lil is given and others are omitted, that indicates the preferred reasoning. This hierarchy ensures consistency and prevents arbitrary selection.

Key Terms
الخانيه (al-Khaniyyah): Fatawa Qadikhan, a famous fatwa collection by Imam Qadikhan.
ملتقى الأبحر (Multaqa al-Abhar): A well-known Hanafi fiqh manual by Ibrahim al-Halabi, often used for fatwa.
المحرر (al-muharrar): The revised, refined version of a legal opinion.
تعليل (ta'lil): Legal reasoning or rationale behind a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are later statements preferred in works like al-Hidayah?
A) They are easier to find.
B) They are the muharrar (revised) version.
C) They are longer and more detailed.
D) They are written by later scholars.

Key Takeaway
Later statements in fatwa works are preferred as muharrar, and statements in Fatawa Qadikhan and Multaqa al-Abhar carry special weight; a single given ta'lil indicates the preponderant reasoning.

The Hierarchy of Hanafi Texts in Fatwa
Establishes hierarchy of Hanafi texts for fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rasm al-mufti hierarchy to determine which textual source is preferred when issuing a fatwa, including exceptions for tas-hih and specific book conventions.

Core Idea
Mutun are preferred over shuruh, and shuruh over old fatwas, unless a later correction (tas-hih) overrides.

This lesson introduces a fundamental rule of Hanafi fatwa methodology: the hierarchy of textual authority. As a mufti, you must know which source takes precedence when multiple rulings exist. The passage establishes that mutun (core texts) are preferred, then shuruh (commentaries), then old fatwa collections. However, explicit corrections (tas-hih) and specific book conventions can alter this order.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default hierarchy
The passage states that mutun are preferred over shuruh, and shuruh over old fatwa collections. This is the baseline for resolving conflicts.
This hierarchy is from those who have authority in tarjih.
2
Recognize the exception for tas-hih
If a wording in a matn is explicitly corrected (sahhaha) by a later authority, the corrected wording takes precedence over the matn.
The corrected wording is considered more preponderant (arjah).
3
Apply specific book conventions
In al-Fatawa al-Khaniyyah and Multaqa al-Abhar, the earlier mentioned statement is preferred. In other books like al-Hidayah, the later mentioned statement is relied upon.
This is because the later statement is the refined (muharrar) conclusion.
4
Understand the role of illa
If jurists provide an illa (cause) for one opinion and neglect the cause for another, the opinion with the illa is preferred.
This indicates a more thorough analysis.
5
Practice applying the hierarchy
Given a conflict scenario, trace through the hierarchy: check if the issue appears in a matn, then a sharh, then fatwa collections; apply tas-hih and book conventions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَكُلُّ قَوْل فِي الْمُتُونِ فَذَاكَ تَرْجِحْ لَهُ ضِمْنَا فَرُجْحَتُ عَلَى الشَّرُوحِ وَالشُّروْحُ عَلَى الْفَتَاوَى الْقِدْمِ مِن ذَاتِ رُجُوحٍ مَا لَمْ يَكُنْ سِوَاهُ لَفْظاً صُحْحَا فَالْأَرْجَحُ الَّذِي بِهِ قَدْ صُرِّحَا ترجمہ: اور ہر وہ قول جس کو متون میں لیا گیا ہے تو یہ اس قول کی ضمنی ترجیح ہے جو اس کو حاصل ہوگئی ہے، پس ترجیح دی جائے گی متون کو شروح پر اور شروح کو قدیم فتاوی پر، یہ ترجیح اصحاب الترجیح کی جانب سے ہے، جب تک متون کے علاوہ قول کی صراحتا صحیح نہ کی گئی ہو، ورنہ وہ قول زیادہ رائج ہوگا جس کی صراحا صحیح کی گئی ہے۔ وَسَابِقُ الْأقْوَالِ فِى الْخَانِيَه وَمُلْتَقَى الْأَبْحُرِ ذُو مَزِيَّهُ وَفِي سِوَاهُمَا اعْتُمِدَ مَا أَخَّرُوا دَلِيلَهُ لأَنَّهُ المُحَرَّرُ كَمَا هُوَ الْعَادَةُ فِى الْهَدَايَه وَنَحْوِهَا لِرَاجِحَ الدِّرَايَهُ كَذَا إِذَا مَا وَاحِداً قَدْ عَلَّلُوا لَهُ، وَتَعْلِيْلَ سِوَاهُ أَهْمَلُوا ترجمہ: اور فتاوی قاضی خان اور ملتقی الابحر میں پہلے بیان کئے ہوئے اقوال فضیلت رکھنے والے ہیں، اور ان دونوں کتابوں کے علاوہ میں اس قول پر اعتماد کیا جائے گا جس کی دلیل فقہاء بعد میں بیان کریں، کیوں کہ وہی قول تنقیح شدہ ہوتا ہے، جیسا کہ ہدایہ اور اس جیسی کتابوں میں یہی طریقہ ہے دلیل کے اعتبار سے راجح قول کے لئے، اسی طرح جب فقہاء ایک قول کی علت بیان کریں اور اس کے علاوہ دوسرے قول کی علت نہ بیان کریں۔
And every statement that is in the texts (mutun) — that is an implicit preference (tarjih) for it, so preference is given to the texts over commentaries (shuruh), and to commentaries over the old fatwa collections — a preference from those who have the authority of tarjih, unless there is another wording that has been explicitly corrected (sahhaha). Then the more preponderant is that which has been explicitly corrected. And the preceding statements in al-Khaniyyah and Multaqa al-Abhar have superiority. And in other than these two, reliance is on what they have delayed (akhkharu) because it is the refined (muharrar) — as is the custom in al-Hidayah and its like for the preponderant understanding. Likewise, when they have given a cause (illa) for one statement and neglected the cause of another.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage presents a rasm rule from the Hanafi usul al-iftaa. It establishes a hierarchical preference: the texts (mutun) are the most authoritative, then the commentaries (shuruh), then the old fatwa collections. This is the default rule, but if a statement in a text has been explicitly corrected (tas-hih) by later scholars, that corrected statement takes precedence over the text's literal wording. The passage then discusses specific books: In al-Fatawa al-Khaniyyah and Multaqa al-Abhar, the earlier mentioned qawl is preferred (because they list opinions in order of strength). In other books, like al-Hidayah, the later stated qawl is preferred because it is the refined, supported conclusion. Similarly, if jurists give an illa (cause) for one opinion and not for another, the opinion with the illa is preferred.

Key Terms
الْمُتُون (al-mutun): The foundational texts of the Hanafi madhhab, such as Mukhtasar al-Quduri, Kanz al-Daqa'iq, etc.
الشُّرُوح (al-shuruh): Commentaries on the mutun, such as Sharh al-Wiqayah, al-Hidayah, etc.
تَصْحِيح (tas-hih): Explicit correction of a wording by a later authority, giving it precedence over the original.
مُحَرَّر (muharrar): The refined, settled position of the school, often the later stated opinion in books like al-Hidayah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The later opinion in time is always preferred.

Key Takeaway
The default hierarchy in Hanafi fatwa is mutun > shuruh > old fatwas, with exceptions for explicit corrections and specific book conventions.

Determining the Mu'tamad and Hierarchy of Corrective Terms
Defines when a corrected qawl is mu'tamad and explains relative strength of terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the mu'tamad qawl when tas-hih occurs and state the relative strength of the terms 'asahh' and 'sahih' in the Hanafi fatwa hierarchy.

Core Idea
When a qawl is explicitly corrected, it becomes the mu'tamad; among corrective terms, 'asahh' is more emphatic than 'sahih'.

In the Hanafi madhhab, not all statements carry equal weight for fatwa. This passage from the rules of tas-hih (correction) explains how to identify the relied-upon opinion (mu'tamad) when there is a correction, and how terms like 'sahih' and 'asahh' indicate the strength of a ruling. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for a mufti to issue a correct fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule for mu'tamad
When you find two qawls and one is explicitly corrected (suhhiha), that corrected qawl becomes the mu'tamad. This is stated at the beginning of the passage.
2
List the terms that indicate a corrected qawl
The passage mentions: al-ashbah (most resembling), al-azhar (most apparent), al-mukhtar (chosen), al-awjah (most appropriate), al-sahih (correct), and al-asahh (more correct).
These are all considered 'sahih' in a general sense.
3
Analyze the relative strength
The passage explicitly states that al-asahh is more emphatic (akad) than al-sahih. There is a minority view that says the opposite, but the majority holds that asahh is stronger.
Look for the phrase 'والأصح آكد منه'.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The fatwa is given according to the stronger qawl. When a qawl is described as 'asahh', it indicates a higher degree of reliability and must be given priority.
The text says: 'كَذَا بِهِ يُفْتَى' — thus fatwa is given upon it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَحَيْثُمَا وَجَدْتَ قَوْلَيْنِ، وَقَدْ صُحِّحَ وَاحِدٌ، فَذَاكَ الْمُعْتَمَدُ. بِنَحْوِ ذَا الْفَتْوَى عَلَيْهِ. الْأَشْبَهُ وَالْأَظْهَرُ وَالْمُخْتَارُ ذَا وَالْأَوْجَهُ الصَّحِيحُ وَالْأَصَحُّ آكَدُ مِنْهُ، وَقِيلَ: عَكْسُهُ الْمُؤكَّدُ. كَذَا بِهِ يُفْتَى عَلَيْهِ الْفَتُوى وَذَانِ مِنْ جَمِيعِ تِلْكَ أَقْوَى:
And wherever you find two statements, and one is corrected (suhhiha), then that is the relied-upon (mu'tamad). The fatwa is upon it accordingly. Al-ashbah (most resembling), al-azhar (most apparent), al-mukhtar (chosen), and al-awjah (most appropriate) are all corrected (sahih), and al-asahh (more correct) is more emphatic (akadu) than it, and it is said: its opposite (i.e., al-asahh is less emphatic?) is [contradicting?]. The fatwa is given upon it. And these two (ashbah and azhar? or asahh and whatever?) are the strongest of all those.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage details the rules for identifying the mu'tamad qawl when tas-hih (correction) is involved. If from two qawls one is explicitly corrected (musahhah), that is the mu'tamad. The fatwa is given accordingly. The passage then lists several terms used by Hanafi scholars to indicate a corrected qawl: al-ashbah (most resembling the principles), al-azhar (most apparent), al-mukhtar (chosen), al-awjah (most appropriate). These are all considered sahih (correct). However, the term al-asahh (more correct) is akadu (more emphatic) than these; some say the opposite. The final line states that the fatwa is given upon it, and these two (likely ashbah and azhar, or perhaps asahh and something else) are the strongest. The commentary clarifies that when a term like 'asahh' is used, it indicates a higher degree of reliability than simply 'sahih'.

Key Terms
الْمُعْتَمَد (al-mu'tamad): The relied-upon opinion in the Hanafi madhhab, which is the basis for issuing fatwas.
الْأَصَحُّ (al-asahh): A term meaning 'more correct', indicating a higher degree of correction than 'sahih'.
الْأَشْبَهُ (al-ashbah): The most resembling (to the principles of the madhhab); a term used to indicate a corrected qawl.
الْأَظْهَرُ (al-azhar): The most apparent; a term indicating a qawl that is clearly correct.
الْمُخْتَارُ (al-mukhtar): The chosen opinion; a term used after tarjih.
الْأَوْجَهُ (al-awjah): The most appropriate; a term indicating a qawl that is preferred due to its appropriateness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Both are equal.
D) It depends on the context.

Key Takeaway
The mu'tamad is the corrected qawl, and among corrective terms, 'asahh' is the most emphatic, so it should be given priority in fatwa.

Identifying the Mu'tamad: Primary Tarjih Terms
Defining the mu'tamad through tarjih terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list at least six Hanafi tarjih terms and state which two are the strongest indicators of the mu'tamad opinion.

Core Idea
When one saying is corrected, that is mu'tamad; the strongest indicators are 'bihi yurfta' and 'alayhi al-fatwa'.

In Hanafi fatwa methodology, not all opinions in the madhhab carry equal weight. Jurists developed a precise vocabulary to signal which view is relied upon (mu'tamad). This lesson unpacks the key terms that a mufti uses to identify the correct opinion when faced with conflicting narrations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage begins with 'Whenever you find two sayings and one has been declared correct...' This is the starting point: two conflicting opinions exist, and one has been explicitly corrected (suhhiha).
The act of 'correcting' (tashih) is performed by later Hanafi authorities.
2
List the tarjih terms
Extract the terms that indicate correction: al-fatwa 'alayhi, al-ashbah, al-azhar, al-mukhtar, al-awjah, al-sahih, al-asahh. These are the linguistic markers that point to the mu'tamad opinion.
Memorize these seven terms; they form the core of Hanafi tarjih vocabulary.
3
Analyze relative strength
The passage notes that 'al-asahh' is more emphatic than 'al-sahih' according to one view, though there is disagreement. Then it states that 'bihi yurfta' and 'alayhi al-fatwa' are the strongest of all the terms.
Remember: direct fatwa phrases outweigh even 'asahh'.
4
Apply to a hypothetical
Given two sayings, if one is described as 'al-azhar' and the other is not corrected, the 'azhar' saying is mu'tamad. If both are corrected, move to the next lesson's rules.
This step reinforces the hierarchy: corrected vs. uncorrected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَحَيْثُمَا وَجَدْتَ قَوْلَيْنِ وَقَدْ صُحِّحَ وَاحِدٌ فَذَاكَ الْمُعْتَمَدُ بِنَحْوِ الْفَتْوَى عَلَيْهِ الْأَشْبَهُ وَالْأَظْهَرُ الْمُخْتَارُ وَالْأَوْجَهُ الصَّحِيحُ وَالْأَصَحُّ آكَدُ مِنْهُ وَقِيلَ عَكْسُهُ الْمُؤَكَّدُ كَذَا بِهِ يُفْتَى عَلَيْهِ الْفَتْوَى وَذَانِ مِنْ جَمِيعِ تِلْكَ أَقْوَى. وَإِنْ تَجِدْ تَصْحِيحَ قَوْلَيْنِ وَرَدْ فَاخْتَرْ لِمَا شِئْتَ فَكُلٌّ مُعْتَمَدٌ إِلَّا إِذَا كَانَا صَحِيحاً وَأَصَحُّ أَوْ قِيلَ ذَا يُفْتَى بِهِ فَقَدْ رَجَحَ أَوْ كَانَ فِي الْمُتُونِ أَوْ قَوْلَ الْإِمَامِ أَوْ ظَاهِرَ الْمَرْوِيِّ أَوْ جُلَّ الْعِظَامِ
Whenever you find two sayings and one has been declared correct (suhhiha), then that is the relied-upon (mu'tamad) by way of phrases like: al-fatwa 'alayhi, al-ashbah, al-azhar, al-mukhtar, al-awjah, al-sahih, and al-asahh is more emphatic (akadu) than it; and it is said the opposite is the emphatic (mu'akkad). Likewise, 'bihi yurfta' and 'alayhi al-fatwa' — and these two are the strongest of all those. And if you find correction of two sayings occurring, then choose whichever you wish, for every one is relied-upon, unless they are sahih and asahh, or it is said 'this is used for fatwa', then it has preponderance, or it is in the texts (mutun) or the saying of the Imam (Abu Hanifa) or the apparent (zahir) of the transmitted (marwi) or the majority of the great scholars.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti rule on how to determine the mu'tamad (relied-upon) opinion when multiple sayings exist in the madhhab. The author lists the key terms used by later Hanafi jurists to indicate tarjih (preference). If one of two sayings has been explicitly corrected (suhhiha) with terms like 'al-fatwa 'alayhi' (the fatwa is upon it), 'al-ashbah' (the more resembling), 'al-azhar' (the more apparent), 'al-mukhtar' (the chosen), 'al-awjah' (the more appropriate), 'al-sahih' (the correct), or 'al-asahh' (the more correct), then that saying is mu'tamad. Among these, 'al-asahh' is more emphatic than 'al-sahih' according to one view, though there is a conflicting opinion. The strongest indicators are the direct phrases 'bihi yurfta' (with it fatwa is given) and 'alayhi al-fatwa' (upon it is the fatwa), which are more powerful than all the others. If both sayings are corrected (suhhiha), then the mufti may choose any of them, as all are mu'tamad. However, there are exceptions: if one is marked as 'asahh' while the other is 'sahih', or if one is explicitly said to be used for fatwa, or if one appears in the primary texts (mutun), or is the direct saying of Imam Abu Hanifa, or is the apparent transmitted view, or is the view of the majority of great scholars, then that saying has preponderance and should be given precedence. This hierarchy helps the mufti navigate conflicting transmissions in the madhhab.

Key Terms
المعتمد (al-mu'tamad): The relied-upon opinion in the madhhab, which the mufti follows
الأشبه (al-ashbah): The opinion that more closely resembles the principles of the madhhab
الأظهر (al-azhar): The more apparent opinion based on textual evidence
المختار (al-mukhtar): The chosen opinion among multiple views
الأوجه (al-awjah): The more appropriate or preferred opinion
الصحيح (al-sahih): The correct opinion according to the madhhab
الأصح (al-asahh): More correct than 'sahih'; considered more emphatic
بِهِ يُفْتِي (bihi yurfta): An explicit statement that fatwa is given with this opinion
عَلَيْهِ الْفَتْوَى (alayhi al-fatwa): An explicit statement that the fatwa is upon this opinion

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which pair of terms is the strongest indicator of the mu'tamad opinion?
A) al-sahih and al-asahh
B) al-ashbah and al-azhar
C) al-mukhtar and al-awjah
D) al-fatwa 'alayhi and bihi yurfta

Key Takeaway
When one of two sayings is corrected with a tarjih term, that saying is mu'tamad; the strongest terms are 'bihi yurfta' and 'alayhi al-fatwa'.

Resolving Between Two Corrected Sayings: Exceptions and Preponderance
Rules for choosing between two corrected opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules for choosing between two corrected opinions, identifying the exceptions that give one opinion preponderance over the other.

Core Idea
When both opinions are corrected, choose any unless one has a stronger term, is in the mutun, is from the Imam, or is the majority view.

The previous lesson established that a single corrected opinion is mu'tamad. But what if both conflicting opinions are corrected? The source passage addresses this directly: the mufti may choose either, unless certain exceptions tip the scale. This lesson examines those exceptions: the presence of a stronger term like 'asahh' or 'al-fatwa 'alayhi', inclusion in the primary texts (mutun), being the direct saying of Abu Hanifa, being the apparent transmitted view (zahir al-marwi), or being the view of the majority of great scholars (jull al-'izam). These factors break the tie and force a specific choice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage says: 'If you find correction of two sayings occurring, then choose whichever you wish, for every one is relied-upon.' This is the default: if both are corrected equally, the mufti has discretion.
'Corrected' here means both sayings have been explicitly validated using tarjih terms.
2
List the exceptions that break the tie
The passage then lists conditions that give one saying preponderance: if one is 'sahih' and the other 'asahh' (or similar stronger term), or if one is explicitly said to be used for fatwa, or if one is found in the primary texts (mutun), or if it is the saying of Imam Abu Hanifa, or if it is the apparent transmitted view (zahir al-marwi), or if it is the view of the majority of great scholars (jull al-'izam).
These exceptions are not alternatives but cumulative factors: any one of them tips the balance.
3
Analyze the exception of 'sahih and asahh'
If one saying is called 'sahih' and the other 'asahh', the one with 'asahh' prevails because it is stronger. Similarly, if one has a direct fatwa phrase ('bihi yurfta'), it overrides even 'asahh'.
The hierarchy: direct fatwa phrase > asahh > sahih > other corrected without a comparative term.
4
Apply to a hypothetical
Suppose you have two corrected narrations on the validity of a gift to a minor. One is found in al-Jami' al-Saghir (a matn) and the other in a later commentary. According to the exception 'or it is in the mutun', the saying found in the primary text takes precedence.
This shows the weight of foundational texts over later commentaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَحَيْثُمَا وَجَدْتَ قَوْلَيْنِ وَقَدْ صُحِّحَ وَاحِدٌ فَذَاكَ الْمُعْتَمَدُ بِنَحْوِ الْفَتْوَى عَلَيْهِ الْأَشْبَهُ وَالْأَظْهَرُ الْمُخْتَارُ وَالْأَوْجَهُ الصَّحِيحُ وَالْأَصَحُّ آكَدُ مِنْهُ وَقِيلَ عَكْسُهُ الْمُؤَكَّدُ كَذَا بِهِ يُفْتَى عَلَيْهِ الْفَتْوَى وَذَانِ مِنْ جَمِيعِ تِلْكَ أَقْوَى. وَإِنْ تَجِدْ تَصْحِيحَ قَوْلَيْنِ وَرَدْ فَاخْتَرْ لِمَا شِئْتَ فَكُلٌّ مُعْتَمَدٌ إِلَّا إِذَا كَانَا صَحِيحاً وَأَصَحُّ أَوْ قِيلَ ذَا يُفْتَى بِهِ فَقَدْ رَجَحَ أَوْ كَانَ فِي الْمُتُونِ أَوْ قَوْلَ الْإِمَامِ أَوْ ظَاهِرَ الْمَرْوِيِّ أَوْ جُلَّ الْعِظَامِ
Whenever you find two sayings and one has been declared correct (suhhiha), then that is the relied-upon (mu'tamad) by way of phrases like: al-fatwa 'alayhi, al-ashbah, al-azhar, al-mukhtar, al-awjah, al-sahih, and al-asahh is more emphatic (akadu) than it; and it is said the opposite is the emphatic (mu'akkad). Likewise, 'bihi yurfta' and 'alayhi al-fatwa' — and these two are the strongest of all those. And if you find correction of two sayings occurring, then choose whichever you wish, for every one is relied-upon, unless they are sahih and asahh, or it is said 'this is used for fatwa', then it has preponderance, or it is in the texts (mutun) or the saying of the Imam (Abu Hanifa) or the apparent (zahir) of the transmitted (marwi) or the majority of the great scholars.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti rule on how to determine the mu'tamad (relied-upon) opinion when multiple sayings exist in the madhhab. The author lists the key terms used by later Hanafi jurists to indicate tarjih (preference). If one of two sayings has been explicitly corrected (suhhiha) with terms like 'al-fatwa 'alayhi' (the fatwa is upon it), 'al-ashbah' (the more resembling), 'al-azhar' (the more apparent), 'al-mukhtar' (the chosen), 'al-awjah' (the more appropriate), 'al-sahih' (the correct), or 'al-asahh' (the more correct), then that saying is mu'tamad. Among these, 'al-asahh' is more emphatic than 'al-sahih' according to one view, though there is a conflicting opinion. The strongest indicators are the direct phrases 'bihi yurfta' (with it fatwa is given) and 'alayhi al-fatwa' (upon it is the fatwa), which are more powerful than all the others. If both sayings are corrected (suhhiha), then the mufti may choose any of them, as all are mu'tamad. However, there are exceptions: if one is marked as 'asahh' while the other is 'sahih', or if one is explicitly said to be used for fatwa, or if one appears in the primary texts (mutun), or is the direct saying of Imam Abu Hanifa, or is the apparent transmitted view, or is the view of the majority of great scholars, then that saying has preponderance and should be given precedence. This hierarchy helps the mufti navigate conflicting transmissions in the madhhab.

Key Terms
المعتمد (al-mu'tamad): The relied-upon opinion in the madhhab, which the mufti follows
الرجحان (al-rujhan): Preponderance, the quality of being stronger or more preferable
المتون (al-mutun): The primary, foundational texts of the Hanafi school (e.g., al-Quduri, al-Jami' al-Saghir)
قول الإمام (qawl al-imam): The direct saying of Imam Abu Hanifa
ظاهر المروي (zahir al-marwi): The apparent meaning of the transmitted reports
جل العظام (jull al-'izam): The majority of the great scholars

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if two corrected opinions exist and one is the saying of Imam Abu Hanifa while the other is from Imam Muhammad, which opinion should be preferred?
A) The saying of Imam Muhammad, because later scholars corrected it.
B) The saying of Imam Abu Hanifa, because the exception 'qawl al-imam' applies.
C) Either, because both are corrected.
D) Neither, because the passage does not give a rule for this.

Key Takeaway
When two sayings are both corrected, the mufti may choose unless one is given preponderance by being the asahh, used for fatwa, in the mutun, the Imam's saying, the apparent transmitted view, or the majority of great scholars.
